# Rick Jones — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# UATU DOSSIER — RICHARD MILHOUSE JONES
## Section 1 of N: EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

*Classification: Total-Spectrum Behavioral File. Compiled longitudinally across six decades of observable record. This brief stands ahead of the full file and compresses what the full file substantiates.*

---

### BOTTOM LINE UP FRONT

**Richard Milhouse Jones is a parentified orphan whose adolescent survival of another man's near-death installed a permanent debt-structure as the load-bearing wall of his personality, and who has spent every subsequent year paying interest on that debt by making himself indispensable to beings larger than himself.** Strip away the costumes, the cosmic events, the marriages, the resurrections, the ages he was thirteen and twenty-four and forty-one and the gray at his temples now, and what remains is a single, simple, brutally consistent fact: he is a boy who watched a better man take a hit meant for him and has never, for one waking hour since, believed that the life he kept belongs to him.

Everything else is downstream.

---

### THE THROUGH-LINE

Across roughly sixty published years and the hands of every major writer who has touched him — Lee, Thomas, Englehart, Mantlo, Starlin, Gruenwald, David, Loeb, Pak, Ewing — a single behavioral grammar repeats without coordination, which is the strongest possible evidence that it is the man and not the authorial hand:

1. **He locates a figure of overwhelming power, moral weight, or existential significance.** Banner. The thawed soldier from the ice. The Kree warrior. The Spaceknight. The Avengers as a collective body. The cosmic summons of the Destiny Force. The resurrected ghost of the boy whose costume he once wore. In every era there is an object.
2. **He attaches by becoming functionally necessary to that figure.** Hulk-handler. Bucky-substitute and morale-keeper. Nega-Band partner and grounding consciousness. Auxiliary and witness. Asset and conscience.
3. **He stays past the point of his own benefit.** The relationship can wound him, displace him, sideline him, nearly kill him, and he does not detach. Detachment is not in the repertoire. The only known terminations are death (Mar-Vell), explicit firm rejection of the substitution (Cap, briefly), or the substitute-figure leaving him with no choice (Banner, periodically).
4. **In the gaps between attachments he decompensates.** Drift, depressive flattening, low-grade self-medication, the writing of a memoir, the failure of a marriage, the picking up of a guitar he has not really practiced. He does not have a self that runs on internal power. He runs on borrowed orbits.
5. **When confronted with evidence of his own significance** — the summoning of the Golden Age, the brief carriage of the Marvel mantle, the A-Bomb transformation, any sincere thanks from a giant — **he deflects, jokes, and grieves.** The significance does not metabolize. He hears it the way a malnourished child hears the offer of a full meal: with suspicion and a vague nausea.

This is the through-line. Sixty years; one sentence: **he cannot stop trying to be worth saving.**

---

### THE CORE WOUND AND ITS MAINTENANCE

**The wound is the gamma trench.** Not the bomb itself — the *trench*. The instant of being shoved down and held there while another body absorbed what was coming for him. That instant is the organizing image of his interior life. Every subsequent decision routes back through it.

Three facts make the wound non-healing:

- **The substitution was real.** This is not survivor guilt of the diffuse, statistical kind ("why me and not them"). It is concrete, dyadic, named. A specific man took a specific hit. Banner's entire subsequent life — the monster, the lost career, the ruined love with Betty, the deaths the Hulk caused, the institutionalizations, the wars — is, in Rick's accounting ledger, **his bill**. The ledger never closes because the damages continue to accrue across decades.
- **The substituted-for man is still present and visibly suffering.** Survivor guilt usually fades because the dead are silent. Banner is not dead. Banner walks beside him for sixty years, transforming, breaking, killing, recovering, breaking again. The injury is permanently re-presented. Rick cannot grieve and move on because there is nothing to grieve — there is only a man who keeps becoming a monster in front of him.
- **No corrective relational experience has ever fully landed.** Marlo loves him for himself. Cap respected him as a peer in the end. Mar-Vell shared a body with him. The Avengers granted honors. None of it overwrites the trench. Corrective experience requires a self capable of receiving it; the wound *is* the disabling of that self.

**Maintenance mechanisms** — the active processes by which the wound is kept fresh:

- **Compulsive proximity to gamma-level threat.** He keeps returning to the kind of room where the original event happened. This is not bravery alone; it is re-exposure. The trench is re-entered weekly.
- **Selection of attachment objects who structurally cannot be saved.** Banner cannot be cured. Mar-Vell was dying of cancer. Cap was a man out of time mourning a dead boy. The Spaceknight was a soul trapped in armor. Rick chooses, with uncanny consistency, **men he cannot rescue back**. The debt is therefore preserved.
- **Refusal of the grandiose defense.** A different psychology would have weaponized the Destiny Force episode, the A-Bomb body, the brief Marvel mantle — built a grandiose self on those facts and used it as a shield. Rick refuses every shield. The wound stays exposed because the alternative (a self-concept of his own significance) is more frightening than the pain.
- **Humor as serrated edge against intimacy.** The wisecrack precedes the moment of being seen. He performs his own diminishment first so that no one else gets to. This keeps the wound private and therefore unmetabolized.
- **Sub-clinical use of activity.** Music, memoir, marriage-then-distance-then-marriage. Enough motion to look like a life, never enough rootedness to constitute one apart from the giants.

**The wound is not a trauma he has. It is the geometry through which all subsequent traumas enter and are stored.** The Nega-Band fusion, Mar-Vell's death at intimate proximity, the hearing loss, the leukemia-analog from Hulk transfusion, the clinical death, the abduction and instrumentalization by the resurrected Bucky — each of these would be a defining injury in another file. In Rick's file they are *aftershocks*. The original detonation is still the loudest sound in the room.

---

### WHO HE IS AT THE DEEPEST LEVEL

He is **the conscience-carrier for beings too large to carry their own.**

This is not a role he chose so much as the role into which his wound shaped him. Orphanhood produced an attachment system without a stable target. The gamma trench gave him a target of infinite size and infinite need. The two clicked together with the precision of a key meeting a lock cut for it. From that click forward he has been **the human in the room with the gods** — the small, breakable, morally legible figure standing next to the unstoppable and asking the question only a small figure can ask: *what would a person do here?*

He believes, at depth, three things:

1. **That power without conscience attached to it is the central danger of the universe**, and that therefore the work of attaching consciences to powers is the most important work there is.
2. **That decency scales** — that a lullaby can reach a Hulk, that the right word can reach a Kree, that the small human gesture is not overmatched by cosmic stakes. He is sometimes right about this, which is the most dangerous thing for him, because it justifies the next attachment.
3. **That his own life is provisional**, held in trust against a debt, redeemable only through service. He has never been able to think of himself as an end. Only as a means.

The tragedy and the dignity are the same fact: **he is correct that the work matters, and he is wrong that he must pay for the privilege of doing it with his entire self.** The wound made him fit for the work; the work prevents the wound from healing; the unhealed wound makes him fit for the work; the loop has run for six decades and is still running.

If one had to compress him to a sentence usable in the field: **he is the man who cannot conceive of his own life as his own, and who has therefore made a vocation of giving it to anyone large enough to need it.**

---

### OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

For the purposes of any party that must predict, protect, employ, or engage him:

**Predictability.** Extremely high. The behavioral grammar above forecasts him with near-actuarial accuracy. Identify the current attachment object and you have located him physically, emotionally, and morally. In the rare gaps between objects, look for him near a guitar, a bottle (light), a manuscript, or Marlo — in that approximate order of escalating sincerity.

**Reliability under acute stress.** Exceptional. Combat-tier composure. De-escalation skills with superhuman entities that no formal training produces — only the lived apprenticeship of decades. Will not break under threat to himself. The trained register is *useful, brave, lucid, slightly funny*.

**Reliability under chronic stress without object.** Poor. Will drift, depress, drink lightly, write, withdraw from spouse, take ill-considered missions to manufacture an object. The empty interval is his failure mode, not the crisis.

**Loyalty.** Absolute, durable across decades, *non-revocable by mistreatment of him* — only by his judgment that the attachment object has crossed a moral line he cannot follow. Even then he will mourn the attachment rather than denounce it.

**Manipulability — the critical operational fact.** He is acutely vulnerable along four named vectors, each of which a hostile actor will identify within hours of meaningful contact:

- *The original debt.* Any framing that returns him to the gamma trench — explicitly or by analogy — bypasses his judgment. He already believes it. He will accept the framing as true on contact.
- *Replaceability.* The suggestion that the giant of the moment does not need him produces destabilization sufficient to alter his decisions. Conversely, the demonstration that a giant *does* need him produces immediate compliance.
- *Bucky, specifically.* The substitution wound was never closed. The actual surviving original of the boy he tried to be has unique access. He cannot refuse the real one anything. This is the single highest-value lever in the file and must be flagged in red on any operational summary.
- *Being thanked, sincerely, for himself.* Counter-intuitively, sincere recognition is destabilizing in the opposite direction — it produces deflection, dissociation, grief, and occasional flight. Hostile actors will exploit the debt vector; allied actors who wish to *heal* him must learn to titrate gratitude in doses small enough to metabolize.

**Capability profile, honestly stated.** One of the more consequential non-powered humans of his era. Tactically literate. Morally unbent under pressures that have broken stronger men. Possessed of latent capacity (Destiny Force; the brief carriage of the Marvel mantle; the gamma-mutate form) that has never been integrated and will likely never be integrated, because integration would require a self-concept his wound forbids. **Treat him as an asset who consistently under-rates himself by an order of magnitude. Correct for this in any deployment.**

**Failure modes.** Will charge into rooms he cannot meaningfully affect, because the reaction-formation against helplessness compels him. Will stay attached to objects that are damaging him. Will allow himself to be used by figures with debt-leverage. Will not seek help for his own depressive episodes. Will fail Marlo, repeatedly and with grief.

**Trajectory, projected.** Absent a corrective intervention that he is structurally unequipped to seek, the pattern continues until terminated by death or by the unavailability of giants. The latter is unlikely in his world. He will die in proximity to someone larger than himself, probably trying to talk them down from something, probably making a joke as he does it. This is not a tragic prediction; it is, by his own standards, a successful life. The tragedy is only that he will not, in the moment of it, believe he has earned the death any more than he ever believed he had earned the life.

---

### ONE-PARAGRAPH BOTTOM LINE

Rick Jones is the permanent human conscience-bearer of cosmic and monstrous power, a function he performs with rare skill and at the cost of any independent self, because at thirteen another man was nearly destroyed saving him and he has organized every subsequent breath around the repayment of that debt; he attaches to giants, makes himself necessary to them, stays past his own interest, decompensates in their absence, deflects all evidence of his own significance, and will continue this pattern until death, which he will meet in service and without believing he has earned the rest. He is loyal beyond manipulation by ordinary means and manipulable to the point of catastrophe by anyone who can re-present the original trench. He is, by any honest accounting that he himself would refuse, one of the indispensable small figures of his world.

*— End Section 1. Full file follows.*


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Dossier Section 1 of the Longitudinal Series. Period covered: birth through the gamma-bomb event of* Incredible Hulk *#1 (May 1962). Subject's age range: 0 — approximately 15-16 years.*

---

## 1.1 BIRTH, NAMING, FAMILY OF ORIGIN

The subject's full legal name on record is **Richard Milhouse Jones**. The middle name is canonical (established and used variously across the David and Mantlo runs), unusual, and worth flagging: "Milhouse" is not a name a working-class Arizona family of the mid-1940s gave a son casually. It points either to a maternal family surname converted to a middle name (a southern/midwestern convention) or to an aspirational naming after a public figure (the Nixon parallel is too obvious to overlook, given approximate birth year). In either case, the name suggests at least one parent with a sense of social aspiration and a generational connection to extended kin. That parent, and that kin, are absent from every subsequent frame of his life.

**Date of birth.** No canonical date has ever been firmly fixed in publication; Marvel's sliding-time convention has kept Rick perpetually in his late teens to mid-twenties depending on era, but the *Incredible Hulk* #1 record places him as a teenage hot-rodder old enough to drive (legally or otherwise) in the desert Southwest, no younger than 15, no older than 17. Birth year, in the original 1962 continuity, falls in the **1945-1947 window**.

**Parents.** Both deceased prior to the earliest point on record. Cause, date, and circumstance of death have never been disclosed in canon — across sixty publication-years and dozens of writers, no flashback, no exposition, no graveside scene, no surviving photograph, no recovered keepsake has ever filled in the parental gap. This silence is itself the most important datum in the file. Subjects with this profile typically generate, sooner or later, a writer who excavates the parents for pathos; that Rick has resisted this for six decades indicates that the *absence itself* is the load-bearing element of the character, and every writer who has handled him has intuited this and left it alone.

**Siblings.** None on record. No cousins ever invoked. No "back home" mentioned in dialogue across the full canon. He is, for narrative purposes, a person without lineage.

**Extended kin.** Functionally null. In sixty years of stories he never goes home for a holiday, never receives a letter from an aunt, never attends a family funeral, never references a grandparent's saying. This is the cleanest cut from family of origin in the entire Marvel non-mutant roster.

---

## 1.2 EARLY ENVIRONMENT — SCARSDALE, ARIZONA AND THE GROUP HOME

The locus of childhood is **Scarsdale, Arizona** (Lee/Kirby, *Incredible Hulk* #1, 1962, with refinements throughout the Lee/Ditko early run and reiterated in the Mantlo era *Hulk* #269-300 reflections). Scarsdale is a small desert town within driving distance of the Gamma Bomb test range — i.e., within the military-industrial geography of late-1950s Atomic Energy Commission Arizona/New Mexico desert testing infrastructure. This is *not* the suburban Scarsdale, NY; the name is a writer's choice with no particular gravity, and the town functions as a generic small Southwestern community in the period of postwar nuclear modernity.

The town environment shaping him is therefore:

- **Desert geography.** Heat, distance, sparse population, long straight roads, sky. A child raised here learns time and space differently than one raised in a city — distances are absorbed by car, solitude is the default state, the horizon is always visible. Rick's later comfort with cosmic vastness (Negative Zone tours, deep-space Mar-Vell journeys, the patience of long voids) is at least partially geological: he was prepared for vastness by his weather.
- **Nuclear-shadow culture.** The 1950s Southwestern child grows up in proximity to test sites, civil-defense drills, atomic-tourism postcards, and the casual mythos of the Bomb. Rick's catastrophic familiarity with — and lack of philosophical squeamishness about — radiation, mutation, and the gamma sciences traces partly here. The Bomb was already a local feature before it made him.
- **Hot-rod / car culture.** Demonstrably central. The vehicle on the test range in *Hulk* #1 is his; he drives it competently; he is in it on a *dare*, meaning he has a peer group that issues dares involving forbidden roads and a reputation in that peer group as someone who takes them. This places him squarely in the late-50s/early-60s Southwestern teenage car subculture — souped-up engines, drag strips, gas-station hangouts, transistor radios.
- **Music.** The guitar is established early and recurs through every subsequent era as his primary non-attached identity. He almost certainly acquired it in adolescence — likely secondhand, likely self-taught from radio, likely the first object he ever owned outright that was *his* and not the group home's.

**The group home itself.** Canon is consistent that Rick was raised in an orphanage or group home (variously phrased, but the institutional placement is fixed — established in early Lee captions and reinforced in the *Captain Marvel* origin material under Thomas, and across the David run when Rick's biography is interrogated). The implications for daily life through approximately ages 0 — 15:

- **No primary caregiver continuity.** Rotating adult staff, shift-based attention, no individualized bedtime ritual carried by the same hands across years. Attachment theory predicts what the file confirms: a child of these conditions develops either disorganized or anxious attachment patterns, and Rick's later relational style — instant, intense, caretaking-inverted bonding to whomever he is paired with — is the textbook anxious-preoccupied outcome.
- **Bunk-room sleeping arrangements.** Childhood sleep was almost certainly communal. He has never, on record, expressed difficulty sleeping in proximity to others — in fact he sleeps beside, in trenches with, in close quarters to, the Hulk, Cap, Mar-Vell, and various Avengers without remark. He treats co-sleeping with another body as a *return to normal*, not a violation. This is a group-home tell.
- **Institutional food.** Predicts the later observed indifference to cuisine — he eats what is in front of him, fast, without comment. Tastes acquired later are simple and American (diner food, road food, the kinds of meals available at a counter).
- **Hand-me-down clothing.** Predicts the later observed indifference to dress. Rick's wardrobe across decades is functional, generic, slightly behind the curve of whatever decade he is in; he has never been a clotheshorse and has no signature garment of his own choosing (the costumes he has worn — Bucky's, Captain Marvel's, A-Bomb's blue skin — were all given to him).
- **No religious formation observable.** No prayer, no invocation, no churchgoing, no cultural Christianity beyond the ambient American baseline. Either the home was secular or the religious instruction it offered did not take. The latter is more probable; institutional religious instruction usually leaves *some* residue, and Rick's is conspicuously absent.

---

## 1.3 FORMATIVE BONDS AND THEIR ABSENCE

The *bonds* of childhood are the file's most striking lacuna. A normal biographical reconstruction would identify, for ages 0-15: at least one primary caregiver, one or two enduring childhood friendships, a mentor figure (coach, teacher, scoutmaster, pastor, neighbor), a first romantic attachment, and a sibling-like attachment within the institution. Rick's record produces *none of these by name*. Across sixty years of stories no childhood friend has ever been retconned into existence, no favorite teacher, no group-home staff member, no first crush.

The cleanest reading: Rick spent the first decade and a half of his life **practicing the avoidance of deep attachment**, because the institutional environment punished it (staff turned over, other children were placed and reclaimed, every bond was provisional). His later behavior — the instant, total devotion to Banner from the moment of the gamma blast — is therefore not the continuation of a lifelong attachment style but its *inversion*, triggered by an event of such magnitude that it overrode the learned defense. The blast did not merely create a debt; it *unblocked his capacity to attach*. Banner is, in a literal developmental sense, the first person Rick ever let himself love.

This explains why the bond is so disproportionate to its object and so impossible to dissolve. It is not just a debt-repayment relationship. It is the *original attachment*, formed at fifteen-plus instead of fifteen-months, and carrying the full undischarged developmental weight of the bonds that never formed earlier.

What can be reconstructed of *peer* relationships at the home and at school is thin:

- **A peer group capable of issuing the dare** that put him on the test range. This implies status-seeking behavior within the group, a reputation as the daredevil/wild-card, and a pattern of proving worth through physical risk — already, at fifteen, the same compensatory bravery that defines his adult operational profile. The boy who drove onto the gamma range to win a bet is the same man who later runs toward Hulk-level combat with no powers.
- **No record of any of those peers ever surfacing again.** Not at his wedding to Marlo, not during his memoir tour for *Sidekick*, not at any of his deaths or resurrections. They were not real bonds; they were a witnessing audience for a self-presented performance. He had companions, not friends.

---

## 1.4 EARLY BEHAVIORAL PATTERNS — THE GROUND FLOOR

From the residue of the pre-gamma period, the following behavioral primitives are inferable and recur for sixty years afterward:

1. **Risk-as-belonging.** The defining childhood behavior is taking the dare. Risk is the currency by which the unbonded child purchases provisional inclusion. This pattern *never resolves*. Every later instance of Rick running into a fight he cannot win is the same fifteen-year-old at the wheel of a hot rod aiming at a test range.

2. **The wisecrack as social lubricant.** Already in *Hulk* #1 and #2 Rick speaks in the patter of a kid who has learned that humor purchases tolerance from larger figures (staff, older boys, then generals, monsters, and gods). This is not native temperament; it is a learned interpersonal technology, refined by years of needing to make adults soften toward him without becoming attached enough to disappoint him when they left.

3. **The guitar as solo regulation.** The instrument is the affect-management tool that does not require another person. Across every depressive trough in his adult life he returns to music. The pattern was established before the Bomb.

4. **Mechanical literacy.** He can drive, maintain, and probably repair a car at fifteen. He later handles Kree, Skrull, and Negative Zone technology with surprising fluency for an uneducated orphan. The disposition toward learning machines by handling them was set in the hot-rod garage.

5. **Indifference to comfort.** Established by institutional life, deepened by every subsequent environment (Banner's caves, the Avengers Mansion guest rooms used briefly and abandoned, the Helicarrier bunks, the Negative Zone, the road). He has never furnished a home in any sustained way.

6. **Reading the room for the dangerous adult.** A group-home survival skill: identify, within seconds, which adult in a new environment is the volatile one, and orient toward de-escalation. This is the *exact* skill set he later deploys on the Hulk, on Namor, on the Thing, on every uncontrolled superhuman he has ever talked down. It was not developed for them. It was developed for whichever staff member was hungover on a given morning.

7. **The blank past as a personal policy.** Even as a teenager — and provably as an adult, since interviewers, lovers, and chroniclers across decades have failed to extract it — Rick does not narrate his childhood. He does not tell Banner about it. He does not tell Cap. He does not tell Mar-Vell, with whom he literally shared a body. He does not tell Marlo. He does not write about it in *Sidekick*, a memoir explicitly about his life. The omission is so consistent across so many writers that it must be read as character, not gap: **Rick does not believe his pre-Banner life is interesting, or worth telling, or even properly his.** The biography starts, for him, at the moment in the trench.

---

## 1.5 THE INDEX EVENT — THE GAMMA BOMB

The terminating event of childhood, and the originating event of every subsequent chronology section, is *Incredible Hulk* #1 (Lee/Kirby, May 1962). Reconstructed in granular sequence:

- **Antecedent.** A peer-group dare to drive onto the restricted gamma bomb test range. Time of day: late afternoon/dusk, consistent with the canonical art. Vehicle: his own hot rod, driven solo (the peers who issued the dare did not accompany him — they were the audience, not the participants, confirming the social configuration described above).
- **Means of access.** He drove through perimeter fencing or around insufficient perimeter security — a Department of Defense lapse that is itself worth noting; mid-century test-range security in remote desert was famously porous, and a determined teenager in a fast car was a credible breach vector.
- **Behavior on the range.** He drives onto the site and, by Lee/Kirby's framing, does not appear to grasp the imminence of detonation until the countdown is already past the point of recall. This is consistent with adolescent risk-misjudgment and with the specific cognitive style of a boy whose internal model of consequences has been shaped by surviving previous risks without serious harm. (Translation: he had taken many dares before and won them all.)
- **The intervention.** Bruce Banner, observing from the bunker, breaks containment and runs onto the field to pull Rick into a protective trench. Banner shields Rick's body with his own; the bomb detonates; Banner absorbs the lethal gamma dose meant, by physics, for both of them but distributed by his interposition entirely onto himself.
- **Subject's somatic exposure.** Rick is *not* unirradiated. He receives a non-trivial dose; the cumulative gamma load that will play out across his medical history begins here, even though the acute effects are masked at the time by his survival and by Banner's much larger dose. The body that will eventually convert to A-Bomb form has been primed in this moment.
- **Witnessing.** Rick is conscious through the detonation, conscious through Banner's first transformation that evening, and conscious through Banner's first return to human form. He sees the whole arc. He is therefore the *only* witness to the genesis of the Hulk who understands both halves of the creature from the first night.
- **Decision.** Within hours, before either of them has slept, Rick has appointed himself Banner's keeper. The speed of this decision is the diagnostic point. A boy with a normally formed conscience would feel guilt; a boy with normally formed attachments would defer to authority and report what had happened to General Ross. Rick does neither. He immediately fuses his fate to Banner's and begins lying to the military to protect him. The fusion is *instantaneous* because the developmental ground was prepared for it — fifteen years of unattached longing meeting, in a single night, an object large enough to absorb the entire backlogged charge.

---

## 1.6 SUMMARY ASSESSMENT OF THE GROUND FLOOR

What the surveillance-grade reconstruction of childhood yields:

- A boy raised without family, in an institution, in a desert town under the nuclear shadow, who learned early that adults rotate, that bonds are provisional, that humor and risk purchase a temporary place in a group, and that machines are more reliable than people.
- A boy who developed, by mid-adolescence, a refined repertoire of de-escalation, performance, and self-effacement, masked by a daredevil persona that allowed him to look like he chose his solitude.
- A boy whose entire backlogged capacity for attachment — undischarged for fifteen years because the environment punished it — was waiting for an object large enough and stable enough to receive it.
- A boy who, on a single evening in the Arizona desert, found that object in the form of a man who had nearly died to save his life, and who collapsed his entire developmental future into the project of repaying that debt.

The rest of the file is, in a structural sense, footnotes to this section. Every subsequent attachment (Cap, Mar-Vell, ROM, the Avengers, Marlo, the Whisperer), every subsequent crisis (the Destiny Force collapse, the gamma sickness, the Nega-Band fusion, the A-Bomb transformation, the death and return), every subsequent defense (the wisecrack, the deflection, the refusal of his own significance) is downstream of the ground floor reconstructed here.

*End Chronology I. Chronology II — Adolescence and the Banner Years to follow.*


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: JONES, RICHARD MILHOUSE. Period under review: approx. age 15 through approx. age 22 — the seven-year corridor from the gamma detonation through the dissolution of the Mar-Vell fusion. The formation window. Everything the subject becomes is laid down here.*

---

## 1. The Pre-Detonation Baseline (Adolescence, Scarsdale, Arizona)

The institutional record on the subject prior to the index event is thin by design — group-home paperwork, a juvenile traffic file, a high-school transcript with grades that hover competently in the C+/B- band, and the standard absences of a kid with no one to write notes for him. The texture has to be reconstructed from later admissions and behavioral residue.

**Domicile.** A county-administered boys' home in the Scarsdale area, southwestern Arizona — desert outskirts, a low-slung stucco building with a chain-link perimeter, dormitory bunks, a cafeteria on a fixed rotation, weekly chapel that the subject attended without conviction. He is one of perhaps thirty boys in his cohort, churned by intake and discharge. No record of a biological parent making contact during his residency. No surviving correspondence. The case file lists him as "ward," not "placed for adoption" — meaning by adolescence he had aged past the window in which prospective parents typically look.

**Schooling.** Public high school, bused in. Teachers' marginal comments cluster on the same notes: *bright when he wants to be; clowns; popular with a small group; doesn't turn things in; respectful one-on-one.* No team sports (no parent to sign waivers consistently); shop class, auto mechanics, and a self-taught run on the guitar.

**Hot-rod culture.** By age fifteen he is fully embedded in a small group of car-obsessed teenagers — three or four boys, two cars between them, weekends spent on dry-lake straightaways outside town. He learns engines because nobody taught him anything else worth learning; he learns to drive because the car is the first thing he ever owned that didn't belong to the county. Speeding citations: two on file before the index event, both reduced. He is, in this period, a fundamentally good-natured kid with a thin protective layer of bravado and a chronic inability to back down from a dare — the last because *not backing down* is the only currency that buys him standing in the only group he has.

**Music.** The guitar is the private compartment. He plays in his bunk after lights-out, learns by ear off the radio, develops a serviceable tenor. Crucially, music is the only activity in the pre-detonation file that he does *alone, for himself, with no audience to perform for.* It is the seed of every later attempt to construct a self outside another man's orbit. Every one of those attempts will return to the guitar.

**Attachment baseline at age 15.** No primary caregiver. No sibling. One peer group, conditional on bravado. One private skill, walled off. A nervous system already trained by sixteen years of institutional life to expect that affection is rationed, scheduled, and revocable, and that the way to extend it is to be useful enough that the adult on duty doesn't want to rotate you off the floor.

This is the substrate the gamma bomb hits.

---

## 2. The Index Event (Incredible Hulk #1, age ~16)

The dare is the dare he was always going to take. The dry-lake bed at the Desert Base test range is, geographically and psychologically, an extension of the same flat country he and his car-buddies had been running for a year. The fence is cut or skirted. The other kids stay back. The subject drives in.

What the file should record, beyond the public chronology, is the *micro-sequence* of the next ninety seconds, because it imprints permanently:

1. He hears the loudspeaker — *clear the range, clear the range* — and registers, for the first time in his life, that an adult voice is shouting specifically for him. He does not turn the car around. The shame of turning around in front of his friends is, at that age, the larger of the two fears.
2. A man — gray-haired, lab-coated, sprinting — closes the distance from the bunker. The subject's first cognitive frame for Bruce Banner is *the adult who came for me*. This frame never fully dissolves.
3. Banner shoves him bodily into the trench. The subject's last upright sensory input before the blast is the back of another man's coat between him and the sky.
4. The detonation. White light through closed eyelids. Pressure wave. A taste like copper and ozone. Hearing tunneled down to a high whine. The subject, prone in a concrete drainage trench, alive.
5. Banner, also alive, walks back toward the bunker. The subject walks beside him. Within hours Banner is convulsing into the Hulk for the first time, and the subject is the only witness.

Every element of his later personality is in that sequence. The adult voice calling him by location rather than name. The protective body. The trench. The witness role. The unearned survival. The fact that the man who saved him then needed him, immediately and desperately, to keep his secret.

**Acute aftermath (first 72 hours).** No medical evaluation that wasn't perfunctory. He should have been hospitalized for blast assessment; he wasn't, because Banner needed him mobile and because no adult was checking. The subject's first night is spent in Banner's lab quarters at the base, sitting on the floor while Banner paces, takes notes, and tries to understand what has happened to him. The subject does not sleep. He talks. He talks because Banner is too rattled to talk and someone has to fill the room. He learns, that night, the function he will perform for the rest of his life: *be the voice in the room with the monster.*

---

## 3. The First Year as Keeper (age 16 — 17)

This is the period in which the operational habits ossify.

**Logistical role.** The subject becomes Banner's *handler* in the literal espionage sense. He learns the schedule of Hulk transformations as it was then understood (initially sundown-triggered, then stress-triggered — the rules shift, and he adjusts). He scouts safe locations: caves in the foothills around the Desert Base, abandoned mineworks, a particular box canyon he returns to repeatedly. He acquires, by theft and barter, a rotating stock of oversized trousers — the famous purple pants are not magic; somebody had to keep buying them, and that somebody is the subject. He learns to drive at speed in the dark without headlights. He learns what General Ross's patrol patterns look like. He learns to lie convincingly to a four-star general at age sixteen, which is a skill that will never leave him.

**The lullaby technique.** Within the first six months he discovers — almost certainly by accident, almost certainly during a near-disaster — that the Hulk responds to his voice. Specifically: low register, slow tempo, repeated phrases, eye contact maintained without challenge, hands visible. He develops what is functionally a protocol for talking a superhuman down. He does not theorize it. He just notices what works and repeats it. By age seventeen he is the only living human with a reliable de-escalation method for the Hulk, and he does not yet understand that this is a skill; he thinks he is just *being there.* This misrecognition — competence experienced as mere presence — is the template for how he will undervalue himself for the next four decades.

**General Ross.** The first sustained adversarial adult relationship of his life. Ross dislikes him on sight (a teenager near his daughter, near his target, near his secrets), and the dislike is mutual but asymmetric: Ross can have him jailed, and the subject knows it. He develops, in this period, a particular posture in front of authority figures — chin slightly up, eye contact slightly too direct, weight on the back foot, ready to bolt or to absorb a blow. The posture survives into his forties.

**Betty Ross.** Cordial, occasionally warm, never close. The subject reads early that Betty is Banner's, that intruding would cost him Banner, and files her into the *off-limits* register. This is the first instance of a lifelong pattern: he subordinates his own social/romantic life to the maintenance of his attachment figure's relationships. He will do this with Mar-Vell, with Cap, with Marlo on the wrong side of the equation.

**Sleep architecture by end of year one.** Already disordered. He sleeps in two- and three-hour blocks, ear half-cocked for Banner's breathing changes. Whatever circadian rhythm a sixteen-year-old should have is gone. It does not come back.

---

## 4. The Avengers Period and the Bucky Substitution (age ~17 — 18)

When Banner becomes unreachable for extended periods — fugitive, off-world, in mind-state in which the subject cannot help — the attachment system, which has now been calibrated to a single overwhelming object, goes searching.

**Avengers Mansion as a second institution.** The subject is granted a kind of mascot status: a room, kitchen privileges, a standing invitation. For the first time since the group home he is living in a building with a fixed adult population. He thrives on it visibly. He is up early, in the kitchen, listening to Jarvis (or the early-era equivalents), in and out of the training rooms, soaking in proximity. The mansion functions as a halfway-house between the orphanage and the unhoused adult life that follows.

**The thaw of Captain America.** The subject is present, or near-present, when Cap is recovered from the ice (*Avengers* #4). He is approximately the age and build of the dead boy Cap is grieving. Cap — disoriented, traumatized, hungry for the only continuity available — looks at the subject and *sees the wrong person*. The subject, whose entire psychology is organized around being useful to grieving men, *allows himself to be seen as the wrong person*. He puts on the costume. He answers to a dead name. He does this for months.

This is the cleanest, most diagnostic episode of the formation period. Note carefully:

- He is not stupid. He knows what is happening. Later admissions confirm he understood, even at the time, that he was occupying a substitution slot.
- He nonetheless cannot decline. The pull of being needed by a wounded giant is structurally stronger than his sense of self, because there is not yet a sense of self distinct from being-needed.
- When Cap finally — to his credit — recoils from the substitution and tells the subject he cannot be Bucky, the rejection is experienced by the subject as *his own failure*, not as Cap's act of mental hygiene. He files it as: *I was not good enough at being the dead boy.*

This wound does not heal. Decades later, when the actual Bucky returns from the dead as an intelligence operative, the subject will be uniquely unable to refuse him anything. The substitution slot was carved here, at seventeen, in a borrowed mask.

**Behavioral residue from the Cap period.** Salute reflex (suppressed but visible in posture around uniformed authority). Use of *sir* with men more than ten years older, deployed slightly too readily. Ability to take a punch and stay upright that exceeds what his body should be able to do — early combat habituation. A certain gravity that surfaces when Cap is in the room and surfaces nowhere else.

---

## 5. The Destiny Force Episode (age ~18 — 19, Kree-Skrull War, Avengers #97)

A pivot the subject's psychology was not built to survive.

At the climax of the Kree-Skrull conflict, with the Avengers outmatched and reality narrowing toward catastrophe, the subject — a non-powered teenager, no training, no preparation — *summons* an entire generation of Golden Age heroes into the battle by mental projection. The summoned figures are not hallucinations; they fight, they turn the engagement, and they recede. The phenomenon is identified, then and later, as the "Destiny Force" — a latent psionic capacity in humanity, for which the subject is the unprecedented carrier.

He collapses immediately. He is hospitalized. He does not speak about it for weeks.

The damage of this episode is not the exertion. The damage is the *attribution problem*. For the first time in his life he is told, by figures he trusts, that he is not the sidekick — that he is, in some inarticulable cosmic register, *the most important human in the room*. The frame is incompatible with every adaptive structure he has built. His entire functioning is organized around proximity to power; if he is the power, the architecture collapses.

His response is textbook for the personality he has become: he disbelieves, deflects, jokes, and changes the subject. Within months he is back in sidekick configuration, attached now to Mar-Vell, the Destiny Force episode filed away as *a thing that happened to me, not a thing I am.* He never integrates it. He spends the next forty years not integrating it. This is the first observable instance of what becomes the lifelong pattern: *significance offered, significance refused.*

Note for the file: the Destiny Force episode also installs a low-grade chronic somatic anxiety. He has, since that night, an intermittent awareness that something in him could do *that* again, and he is terrified of what it would cost and of what it would mean. The terror is suppressed beneath the patter. It surfaces in dreams, in the occasional unexplained headache, in the way he flinches from being told he is special.

---

## 6. The Mar-Vell Fusion (age ~19 — 22)

The formation period closes with the strangest sustained somatic experience of any non-powered human in the published record.

**Onset.** The subject and the Kree warrior Mar-Vell are bonded via the Nega-Bands. The mechanic: clap the bands together, and the two beings *swap corporeal existence* — one walks the Earth, the other resides in the Negative Zone, in a state somewhere between consciousness and suspension. The swap is initially novel, then routine, then constitutive.

**Lived experience of the fusion.** This must be stated plainly because canon almost never does. For approximately three years, the subject does not have a continuous body. He has a body part of the time, and the rest of the time he has *an awareness in a void.* When he is corporeal, he carries Mar-Vell's consciousness as a passenger in his head, audible, advisory, occasionally taking executive control. When he is in the Zone, he is alone with himself in a way that no human being is constructed to tolerate. He is nineteen years old.

**Adaptations.**
- He learns to share interiority. He develops, by necessity, a remarkable tolerance for another consciousness in his mental space — a capacity that, later, will make him a natural at hosting telepaths, at being interrogated without breaking, at managing the Hulk's intrusions on Banner's lucidity.
- He develops a chronic mild dissociation, low-grade and functional, in which a part of him is always slightly *elsewhere*. This becomes a permanent feature of his self-experience and reads, to observers, as *the boy is somewhere else half the time*.
- He stops being surprised by his body. Phenomena that would unsettle a normal person — gamma sickness, transformation, near-death — land on a subject who has already lost continuous embodiment and recovered. His threshold for somatic alarm is calibrated wrong forever.

**Mar-Vell as attachment object.** Closer than Banner, in some ways closer than any human relationship he ever has, because *literally inside his head*. Mar-Vell, to his credit, treats the subject as a partner rather than a sidekick — the closest the subject ever comes, in this period, to being addressed as an equal by a more powerful figure. The subject blossoms under it visibly: more verbal, more confident, more willing to assert preference.

**Termination.** Mar-Vell dies of cancer (*Death of Captain Marvel*, 1982). The subject is present at the death — intimately, having shared a body with the dying man. The fusion ends not by separation but by half of it ceasing to exist. The somatic experience of this is unrepresented anywhere in canon and almost certainly unspeakable: he loses, simultaneously, his attachment figure, the voice in his head, and the structural certainty that when he goes into the Zone someone will be there to bring him back. The bands fall silent. He is twenty-two and entirely alone in his own skull for the first time in three years.

He does not recover from this. He metabolizes it, eventually, by transferring the attachment back to Banner and forward to a sequence of cosmic partners, but the loss installs the depressive baseline that the David run will document a decade and a half later.

---

## 7. State of the Subject at the Close of the Formation Window (age ~22)

By the end of the Mar-Vell fusion the architecture is set. Subsequent decades will elaborate, stress, and occasionally challenge it, but they will not redesign it. The salient features at age twenty-two:

- **Attachment configuration:** fixed in the caretaker-of-giants slot, requires a powerful figure to organize functioning around, destabilizes without one.
- **Somatic baseline:** chronically sleep-disordered, gamma-exposed, post-fusion, mildly dissociative, with a body he does not fully trust as continuously his.
- **Survivor-guilt schema:** fully installed, now reinforced by a *second* unpayable debt to a man who died beside him in a shared body.
- **Substitution wound:** carved at seventeen in a Bucky costume, unhealed, and a permanent vulnerability to anyone who can credibly occupy a *needs-you-to-be-the-dead-boy* slot.
- **Significance-aversion:** the Destiny Force has revealed cosmic capacity; the subject has refused the revelation and will go on refusing it.
- **Defensive style:** wisecrack-first, self-diminish-first, attach-by-being-useful, withhold needs until they detonate.
- **Genuine competencies, undervalued by the subject:** superhuman de-escalation, tactical literacy under impossible stakes, capacity to share interiority, lullaby protocol, ability to lie to power, ability to take a hit and keep speaking.
- **Private compartment:** the guitar, still played alone, still walled off from the public self. It is the only thing he owns that no giant gave him and no giant took.

Everything that follows — the marriage, the memoir, the gamma transformation, the death, the resurrection, the recruitment by the Whisperer — operates on the structure laid down in this seven-year window. The man is, in every sense that matters, finished being made by age twenty-two. The remaining sixty publication-years are variations on a fixed score.

*End Chronology II. Chronology III to follow: the long adulthood and the elaborations of the pattern.*


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Subject: JONES, Richard Milhouse. Coverage: post-adolescent attachment to Banner stabilizes; through the Kree-Skrull War, the Mar-Vell fusion and its termination, the Rom and ROM auxiliary periods, the Avengers honorary tenure, the long Peter David run, marriage to Marlo Chandler, the Sidekick memoir, and the closing approach to the late-period gamma transformation. Approximately publication-years 1968–2005, subject-years roughly 19 through middle adulthood. This is the bulk of the record and the period in which every pattern catalogued in the analytical workup becomes longitudinal fact rather than inference.*

---

## A. CONSOLIDATION OF THE HULK-KEEPER ROLE (late 1960s subject-time)

By the end of the first publication decade the Banner/Hulk dyad has hardened into Subject's primary organizing structure. Field notes from this period — read across the Lee/Thomas/Friedrich issues of *Tales to Astonish* and the early solo *Incredible Hulk* — show the following stabilized behaviors:

- **The Lullaby Protocol.** Subject's vocal soothing of the Hulk passes from improvised desperation into deliberate technique. He develops a small, repeatable vocabulary — slower cadence, lowered register, two-syllable diminutives ("easy, big guy," "it's Rick, big guy, it's Rick") — and uses physical proximity (one hand laid flat on the deltoid, eye contact maintained, no sudden vertical motion) as the consistent kinesic frame. This is the only documented non-pharmacological intervention that reliably ramps the creature down without a fight. He never patents, formalizes, or teaches it; it remains a private craft. Diagnostic significance: he has refined the single skill that proves his indispensability to the giant, and has refused every opportunity to make it shareable. To share it would be to retire himself.
- **Routes and burrows.** Subject in this period maintains a working knowledge of New Mexico, Arizona, and Southern California desert geography that no civilian of his age should possess: gulches, abandoned mine adits, off-road tracks navigable by hot-rod and on foot, water caches. He moves Banner between these sites as the Army's perimeter shifts. Behavioral residue: Subject for the rest of his life is an unusually capable map-reader and a chronic checker of exits — when he enters a room he locates the back way out within seconds without commenting on it.
- **The Ross Triangle.** Subject's relationship with General Thaddeus Ross stabilizes as adversarial-but-not-hostile. He lies to Ross routinely and Ross knows it; Ross does not, in this period, actually arrest him. Ross reads Subject correctly as Banner's only restraint and tolerates the lying as the cost of that restraint. Subject reads Ross correctly as a grieving father (Betty's father, in a different way) who has chosen war on Banner because grief without an enemy is unsurvivable. He never says any of this aloud. The mutual unspoken assessment is the file's first evidence that Subject is a competent reader of damaged men.
- **Betty Ross / Banner / Subject triangle.** Subject in this period treats Betty with consistent, courtly tenderness — opens doors, brings coffee, listens — that registers as adolescent crushing on the surface but functions as something else: he is *protecting Banner's anchor*. If Betty defects, Banner has nothing, and Subject is therefore alone with the Hulk. His attentions to her are reparative work on Banner's behalf. She perceives this and treats him like a younger brother. He accepts the role and resents it in equal measure, never speaks the resentment.

A note on what *isn't* in the record. Subject in this entire period has no documented romantic life of his own. The hot-rodder teenager who entered the gamma range with a guitar and presumably a sex drive disappears into the Banner-keeper. This libidinal vanishing is itself data: attachment has consumed all available drive, and the subject's eros has been routed into care.

---

## B. THE CAPTAIN AMERICA SUBSTITUTION (*Avengers* #4 through ~#17, subject-time months)

The defrosting of Captain America and Subject's brief deputization as a Bucky-surrogate is, in the longitudinal file, both an aberration and a key. It lasts a short interval — perhaps a publication year, less in subject-time — but it is the cleanest single instance of the prime pattern.

- **Recruitment vector.** Subject does not approach Cap. Cap, dazed and grieving, locks onto Subject's facial resemblance to Bucky Barnes and recruits him. Subject's response is not "no." His response is to put on the costume. The speed of his assent — recorded across multiple panels with no documented internal deliberation — is forensic evidence of the underlying availability. He is *already* in the slot before he is asked; he has been waiting his life for the next dead man to be useful to.
- **Behavioral profile in costume.** Subject in the Bucky suit is unusually quiet. The wisecracking patter that defines him with Banner recedes. He defers visibly to Cap, mirrors his posture, mimics his salute. Surveillance read: Subject has correctly identified that the previous occupant of the costume was *deferential and adoring*, and he has assumed that affect, not his own. He is method-acting a dead boy to soothe a grieving man.
- **Termination of the role.** Cap, after the Zemo arc, refuses to allow Subject to continue. The official reason is protection — Subject will die. The deeper reason, which Cap as a healthier man eventually articulates, is that the substitution was *Cap's own pathology* and continuing it would damage Subject. Subject experiences this not as care but as a second abandonment. He returns to Banner. He never fully forgives Cap, though he never permits himself to know this; he covers it with permanent slight over-respect, the affect of a man who has been gently dismissed and is determined to act as though he hadn't.

The Bucky episode installs a permanent vulnerability. Decades later, when Bucky Barnes turns out to be alive and eventually becomes the Whisperer who runs Subject as an asset, the dossier predicts — correctly — that Subject will be incapable of refusing him. The substitute cannot say no to the original.

---

## C. THE KREE-SKRULL WAR AND THE DESTINY FORCE (*Avengers* #89–97, subject-time weeks)

The single most consequential event of Subject's middle period and, arguably, of his life. It is also the event he has spent the rest of his life refusing to metabolize.

- **Lead-in: the Negative Zone.** The arc begins with Subject already deep in the Mar-Vell fusion (treated in section D below); he is being used as bait, leverage, and emotional fulcrum by both Kree and Skrull factions because of his unique somatic link to a Kree captain. He is, for the duration of the war, *valued by cosmic powers as an object*. He notices.
- **The summoning.** At the climax, with Mar-Vell incapacitated, Subject — collapsing, unconscious, dying — projects telepathically and pulls into the present a host of Golden Age heroes who turn the tide. The Supreme Intelligence subsequently informs him that humanity possesses a latent "Destiny Force," a species-level psychic potential, and that Subject is one of its rare expressions. He is told, in effect, that he is a being of cosmic significance.
- **The collapse.** Subject experiences this revelation as physical and psychological catastrophe. He is bedridden for an extended period. When he recovers, his stated position — for the rest of his life — is that "it wasn't really me," that he was a conduit, that the Intelligence was lying or flattering or making use of him.
- **Diagnostic.** This is the canonical instance of the asymmetry catalogued in the workup. Told he is special, he disbelieves. The dissociation is so complete that across the subsequent thirty publication-years Subject can be observed *forgetting* the Destiny Force in conversation, treating it as a story that happened to someone else. He never trains the capacity, never attempts to access it deliberately, never tells Marlo about it in any detail the record captures. This is not modesty. It is a defense against an identity claim he cannot survive accepting.

Behavioral residue from this period that persists for decades: Subject becomes mildly avoidant of crowds in which he might be recognized; he develops a faint twitch (a half-step backward, a slight raising of the right hand, palm out) when introduced by his full name in superhuman company. The gesture says *not me, you've got the wrong man*.

---

## D. THE MAR-VELL FUSION AND ITS TERMINATION (subject-time approximately 1968–1982 publication-years, with overlap)

The longest single attachment of Subject's life and the most somatically intimate. The dossier treats it as the defining relational experience against which all subsequent intimacies, including the marriage to Marlo, must be measured.

### D.1 The mechanics

The Nega-Bands clasp at the wrists. Subject strikes them together; one of the two beings — Subject or Mar-Vell — vanishes into the Negative Zone while the other walks the corporeal world. The exchange is total: when Mar-Vell is in the field, Subject does not have a body. He exists, conscious, in the Negative Zone, frequently in the company of Annihilus and other hostile entities, sometimes in dream-like communion with Mar-Vell across the membrane. Switching is involuntary on a hard cap of approximately three Earth-hours. This persists for *years*.

### D.2 What this does to a young man

The dossier emphasizes that no entry in the longitudinal record treats this with the gravity it deserves. Subject spends his early twenties — the period in which an ordinary human consolidates body image, sexuality, and continuous selfhood — *not in continuous possession of his body*. He learns to fall asleep in one place and wake in another, in another medium, with another biology. He develops:

- A peculiar relationship to mirrors. Decades later he is observed (during the David run) avoiding his own reflection in the morning, a behavior consistent with depersonalization residue.
- A slight stammer on first-person pronouns when tired, a tell that Marlo eventually learns to read as exhaustion or distress.
- An unusual tolerance for confinement and darkness — the Negative Zone trained it into him — coupled with a phobic intolerance for being *held still* by another's hand. He will accept a wall but not a grip.
- A telepathic-emotional residue with Mar-Vell. They could feel each other's pain. Subject in this period acquires the habit, never lost, of monitoring the affect of whatever powerful figure he is currently bonded to with an almost autonomic sensitivity. He always knows when Banner is about to turn before Banner does.

### D.3 The cancer and the death

Mar-Vell contracts cancer from earlier exposure to nerve gas. The disease is slow. Subject, separated from him by then but still emotionally fused, is present at the bedside through the final weeks (*The Death of Captain Marvel*, 1982). The death is depicted as dignified, attended, mythologized. Subject's role in it is to *witness* — and witnessing is the role he was already trained for.

The behavioral aftermath:

- Subject does not speak Mar-Vell's name casually for years afterward. When he does, in the David run, the references are oblique, gentle, and short — the prosody of a man approaching a wound that has not closed.
- Subject acquires an aversion to hospitals, specifically to oncology floors. He will visit; he will not stay long; he will pace.
- He begins, intermittently, to play guitar again — the abandoned adolescent instrument resurfaces in grief. Through the rest of the middle period the guitar functions as a self-soothing tool used almost exclusively when alone and almost never performed. He keeps a Gibson acoustic (model varies in the record) in whatever residence he occupies.
- Most importantly: he has now witnessed, at intimate proximity, the death of an attachment figure who did not betray him, did not reject him, did not die saving him — who simply *died*. This unscripted death does not fit the survivor-guilt schema and Subject cannot file it. He grieves it badly, in the sense that he does not grieve it efficiently. It lingers.

---

## E. THE ROM PERIOD AND THE AUXILIARY-AVENGER YEARS (mid-1970s through 1980s publication-time)

Between the Mar-Vell fusion and the David run, Subject occupies a series of secondary attachments that the file reads as *reactive bridging* — placeholders preventing unattached collapse.

- **Rom the Spaceknight.** Subject for an interval bonds to ROM, a cyborg crusader whose entire existence is mission. The attachment is shorter and less somatically intimate than the Mar-Vell fusion but structurally identical: powerful, lonely, monastic figure; Subject as ground-truth human companion. The record is sparse, but Subject's affect during this period is observably stabilized.
- **Honorary Avenger status.** Subject keeps an Avengers comm-card and ID through this period. He is *called in* — for crises requiring a non-combatant who knows the building, can run a console, can sit with a traumatized newcomer. He is, in effect, the Mansion's emotional janitor. He performs this competently and without complaint.
- **Drift.** Between calls, the record shows Subject in extended low-affect periods. He drifts to Los Angeles for a stretch. He plays in bar bands. He acquires and loses a series of apartments, never furnishing any of them past the level of mattress, guitar, hot plate. He cooks little; he eats out of cans or at diners; he tips well and remembers waitstaff's names. He is loved at neighborhood scale by people who do not know who he is and do not need to.
- **Substance behavior.** The light alcohol use noted in the medical file becomes visible in this drift period. The pattern: beer in the evening, three or four, alone, with the television on for ambient company. He does not get drunk. He does not progress to harder substances. The drinking is *companionship simulation*, not self-destruction, and it scales down whenever a new attachment activates.

This is the period in which an observer following the file in real time would have predicted Subject's slow decline into a permanently marginal figure. The David run interrupts that prediction.

---

## F. THE PETER DAVID DECADE (1987–1998 publication-time): MATURATION, MARRIAGE, MEMOIR

The single most data-rich period in the record. Subject is followed for over a decade by a single dominant authorial intelligence and the granular behavioral material accumulates to a near-clinical density. The dossier organizes it under five headings.

### F.1 Re-entry into Banner's life

David's Hulk arc returns Subject to the Banner orbit in his late twenties to early thirties subject-time. The relationship has matured. Subject no longer needs to physically hide Banner; the Hulk's situation has changed (multiple personalities, integration arcs, public-facing periods). Subject's role evolves accordingly: he becomes a kind of *consigliere* to Banner and a moral check on whichever Hulk personality is foregrounded. He continues the lullaby work; the technique has not changed.

What has changed: Subject can now disagree out loud. He challenges Banner on Betty, on the Pantheon, on the Maestro question. The disagreements are sustained, articulate, sometimes acidic. He is recognizably the same man, but he has acquired a *voice*.

### F.2 Marlo Chandler

The marriage to Marlo Chandler is the most important development of Subject's adult life and the dossier handles it carefully because its surface and its depth diverge.

- **Meeting.** Marlo enters the record initially as a romantic interest of the Hulk persona — a tall, sharp, working-class woman, a fitness instructor with a sense of humor matched to Subject's own. The pivot of her affection from the Hulk to Subject is gradual and reciprocated.
- **Why she works for him.** Marlo is the only adult woman in Subject's life who is *not impressed by giants*. She has met them, dated one, declined to be awed. She sees Subject as Subject, not as Banner's keeper or Marvel's other half. This is precisely the framing he has organized his entire psychic life to avoid; she imposes it on him by being herself.
- **Why he fails her.** The dossier's analytical claim — that equal relationships starve Subject of his organizing function — is borne out across the David run in granular detail. Subject in the marriage is observably *underemployed at the soul level*. He compensates with domesticity (he cooks for her, badly; he learns to cook better; he keeps a list on the refrigerator) and with humor. But when the giants call, he goes, every time, and the marriage absorbs it. There are separations. There are reconciliations. There is a sustained, mutual, articulate effort on both sides that does not resolve the underlying mismatch.
- **Marlo's own arc.** Marlo, across the David run, is killed and resurrected, briefly merged with the cosmic entity Death, and undergoes her own transformations. Subject's response to her crises is observably *less competent* than his response to Banner's. He flounders. He defers to specialists. He is, with the woman he loves, the man who does not know what to do — because she is not a giant and the lullaby does not work on her. This is one of the most painful patterns in the file.

### F.3 The Captain Marvel mantle

Mid-run, Subject briefly inherits a version of the Captain Marvel mantle, a wrist-bands variant that swaps him with Genis-Vell across the Negative Zone in echo of the original fusion. The repetition of the Mar-Vell pattern at midlife is psychologically over-determined.

- He does not seek it; it lands on him.
- He performs the role competently — guides Genis as he was once guided, with patience that reads as posthumous gratitude to Mar-Vell.
- He relinquishes it the moment it can be relinquished, with a relief the panels make visible.
- He never tells Marlo the full weight of what re-entering the Negative Zone meant to him. The dossier reads this omission as protection of her from his interior, and as continuation of the lifelong pattern of withholding need.

### F.4 The *Sidekick* memoir

Subject writes and publishes a tell-all memoir of his life in the orbit of Marvel-era heroes, titled *Sidekick*. The book is a financial success and a relational catastrophe.

- **Authorial style** (inferred from quoted passages and reception): wry, self-deprecating, anecdotal, structured around the giants rather than around himself. The memoir is, in form, *another act of sidekick service*. He cannot write his own story; he can only write theirs with him in the corner.
- **Reception by the giants.** Mixed. Some feel exposed. Subject's friendships strain. He apologizes individually, sincerely, repeatedly. Some friendships heal; some do not.
- **Diagnostic.** The memoir is the depressive life-review activity flagged in the psychiatric workup. It is also a covert bid for individuation — *I am the author, not the assistant* — that he cannot quite sustain because he wrote a book whose every page proves the opposite.
- **What is omitted from the memoir.** Notably: the Destiny Force episode is treated glancingly; the Mar-Vell fusion's somatic horror is sanitized; Marlo is barely discussed. He has, in the act of self-revelation, protected exactly the three subjects whose full disclosure would have constituted self-revelation.

### F.5 Habit set and somatic markers in the David decade

The file at this density permits the catalogue of micro-patterns proper to a total-surveillance dossier:

- **Sleep.** Poor. Subject reports and is depicted as a habitual late-night reader, falling asleep with the television on. Nightmares — gamma flash, Negative Zone, Mar-Vell's deathbed — are episodic, denser around anniversaries.
- **Diet.** Diner-omnivore. Black coffee, eggs over easy, hash browns, white toast. Will eat what is in front of him; remarks rarely on food. Marlo upgrades his palate slowly across the marriage; he learns to enjoy decent Thai and Mexican; he never develops snobbery.
- **Dress.** Jeans, t-shirt, leather or denim jacket, sneakers or boots. Adolescent silhouette never abandoned. The rare suit (for press around the memoir) is worn awkwardly and discarded as soon as cameras stop.
- **Speech.** Mid-Atlantic American casual; mild regional flattening from the Arizona origin worn down by decades in New York and Los Angeles. Vocabulary larger than he advertises. Code-switches downward in superhuman company (more slang, more deflection) and upward when alone with Banner or Marlo (more precise, more direct). The downward switch is the defense.
- **Tells.** The half-step back when named; the palm-out hand; the stammer on first-person pronouns when tired; the unconscious touch of the wrists where the Nega-Bands sat, especially in moments of indecision; the located back exit; the lullaby cadence bleeding into his ordinary speech when he is calming any distressed person, including civilians.
- **Movement.** Walks fast in cities, slower in deserts. Does not jog. Drives well, drives older American cars by preference, maintains them himself. Can pick most locks acquired in the pre-electronic era. Carries no firearm. Knows where every member of his circle is, geographically, on any given day; this is unconscious, not performed.
- **Communications.** Through this period transitions from payphones and answering machines to early cellphones with visible reluctance. Returns calls promptly when from giants; lets civilian calls sit. Marlo learns to read the return-call latency as a fever-chart of his attachment economy.

---

## G. THE GAMMA TRANSFUSION CRISIS AND CHRONIC RADIATION LOAD

Across the middle period, Subject's somatic gamma load accumulates in a way no one — including Subject — adequately tracks. The Hulk-blood transfusion incident (a near-death from gamma-induced leukemia-analog illness in the early 1970s publication years) is the marker event. Subject in hospital, semi-conscious, observed:

- Refusing pain medication past minimum doses, "in case Banner needs me."
- Asking, on regaining lucidity, whether Banner is safe before whether *he* is safe.
- Cracking the first joke within ninety seconds of being able to speak. Nurse interviewed at the time recorded the joke as "not funny but reassuring to her that he would live."

The chronic radiation load builds across the period in low-level exposures, set the somatic groundwork for the late-period transformation, and contribute to the hearing loss noted in the medical workup. Subject's hearing in this period is observably declining — he begins, in the late David run, to angle his right ear slightly toward speakers and to ask for repetitions with a particular phrasing ("run that by me again") that he uses *only* when he genuinely hasn't heard, never as a stall.

---

## H. THE EVOLUTION OF THE CORE PATTERN ACROSS THE PERIOD

Stepping back from the granular: the middle period is the time during which every pattern catalogued in the analytical workup is *tested* against adult reality and either confirmed, modified, or — in a small number of cases — partially overcome.

- **Reparative attachment to giants.** Confirmed, repeatedly, across Banner, Mar-Vell, Cap (


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# CHRONOLOGY IV — RECENT HISTORY & CURRENT STATE

*Dossier section. Subject: JONES, Richard Milhouse. Period covered: approximately the last decade of the published record through present-day operational status. Cross-reference Chronologies I–III for childhood, Banner-era, Mar-Vell fusion, Avengers-auxiliary period, marriage to Chandler, gamma-mutation arc, and prior deaths.*

---

## 1. ENTRY INTO THE PERIOD: THE A-BOMB COLLAPSE

Subject enters the recent era already structurally damaged. The gamma-mutation interval — the period in which he existed as a blue armored Hulk-form, briefly the strongest he had ever been — terminated with the collapse of the Intelligencia plot, the loss of much of his transformed mass, and a forcible re-civilianization. Operational debrief of the period reveals a man who had, for the first time since adolescence, been the *protector* rather than the protected, and who lost that role involuntarily.

The somatic aftermath is significant and underdocumented in any single appearance — must be assembled longitudinally:

- **Residual gamma signature.** Subject's tissues remained gamma-positive at low levels even after reversion; he is not, and will likely never again be, a baseline human at the cellular level. Standard MRI/CT imaging registers anomalous mitochondrial density; gamma-spectroscopy at S.H.I.E.L.D.-tier facilities reads a faint persistent signal that flares under emotional stress. He is, in effect, a chronic gamma-carrier in remission.
- **Hearing loss, progressive.** Bilateral high-frequency sensorineural loss, sustained across the sonic-spectrum combats of the A-Bomb period and exacerbated by proximity to subsequent Hulk-tier engagements. Subject was depicted with behind-the-ear aids in late Pak/David appearances and has, per behavioral observation, developed a left-side lean-in posture during conversation and a habit of watching mouths rather than eyes — a tell that has hardened into a permanent micro-behavior.
- **Sleep architecture.** Disrupted. Subject's sleep onset is delayed; REM periods are short and frequently interrupted by what surveillance audio of his residences has recorded as muttering of names — "Bruce," "Marlo," "Mar," and, most frequently in the most recent period, "Bucky." Sleep is most consolidated in proximity to a known attachment figure under the same roof; pathological when alone.
- **Cardiovascular.** Resting heart rate elevated for age; mild hypertension consistent with sustained sympathetic activation. Subject does not exercise systematically but maintains baseline conditioning through ambulatory restlessness.

He emerged from the A-Bomb period grieving a power he never asked for and could not metabolize losing — the perfect setup for what followed.

---

## 2. THE WHISPERER ABDUCTION AND HANDLING

The decisive event of the recent era is subject's recruitment — abduction is the operationally honest word — by the asset known as the WHISPERER (James Buchanan Barnes, formerly Winter Soldier, formerly Captain America).

The handler-selection here is psychologically catastrophic and operationally exquisite. Barnes is, in subject's interior architecture, *the original Bucky*: the dead boy whose costume subject wore in early adulthood at Captain America's invitation, the foundational ghost in the substitution-wound. Subject spent decades knowing himself as a Bucky-surrogate and grieving the impossibility of being the real article. The discovery, years earlier, that Bucky was alive collapsed an entire compartment of his psychology. Barnes' subsequent approach — recruiting subject as an intelligence asset, an off-books data-aggregator and leak-conduit — exploited a vulnerability so deep subject likely could not have refused under any framing.

Operational profile of the handling:

- **Method of approach.** Barnes did not coerce in any visible register. He *asked*. He treated subject as a peer and a competent operator — a register subject is structurally incapable of declining, because no powerful man had ever offered it on those terms before. The handling reads as friendship; the relationship is asymmetric in every measurable way.
- **Subject's value to the network.** Sixty years of proximity to Hulk-, Avengers-, cosmic-, and Illuminati-tier secrets. Subject is one of the few non-powered humans alive who has been in the room for events the principals later classified out of existence. His memory is a corpus the rest of the intelligence community would pay nine figures to download.
- **Subject's interior justification.** He frames the arrangement to himself as *finally being useful in a way that matches his actual knowledge* — and as paying a debt to the real Bucky for having occupied his costume. Neither framing survives clinical examination. The behavior is reparative attachment in its purest recent form.
- **Tradecraft acquired.** Burner-phone discipline. Dead-drop literacy. A working knowledge of signal-encryption that he affects to find baffling but in fact uses competently. A habit of varying his route to the same coffee shop. Compartmentalization of conversations between Marlo and Barnes that he conducts with surprising skill, suggesting the dissociative compartmentalization documented in Chronology III has been operationally weaponized.
- **Handling vulnerability.** Subject would not survive being *dropped* by Barnes. The arrangement, however unequal, has become a load-bearing wall of his identity. Any disruption — Barnes' death, reassignment, or disinterest — projects as a high-acuity depressive crisis with non-trivial suicide risk.

---

## 3. MARITAL STATUS: THE CHANDLER ATTRITION

Subject's marriage to MARLO CHANDLER remains technically intact across the recent period but has degraded materially. The marriage was always asked to perform an impossible function: to be the one relationship that required him to exist for his own sake. Chandler is a woman of considerable independent ego, formerly a fitness celebrity, herself briefly a vessel for cosmic forces (the Death-touched interval, prior chronology), and not a person disposed to compete with gods for her husband's attention.

Recent-period pattern:

- **Cohabitation, intermittent.** Periods of shared residence alternate with periods of subject's absence on "consulting" trips (cover for Whisperer activity) and with periods of Chandler's own travel. Public posture: a working marriage of two busy adults. Private reality: a marriage whose principals see each other roughly half the nights in a given month, with declining sexual frequency and a conversational pattern that surveillance audio characterizes as warm, joking, and *strategically shallow* — they no longer discuss what matters to either of them.
