Subject walks daily, mid-to-late afternoon, between forty-five minutes and two hours. The route is **not exercise** — it is patrol disguised as habit. He prefers the same loop within any given neighborhood, varies the direction (clockwise one day, counterclockwise the next; a behavior with no civilian rationale), and **maps egress** without realizing he is doing so. He clocks rooftops, alleys, vehicles repeated across days. He acquired this behavior the year he and Banner first went to ground in 1962 and has never lost it.

He stops at one or two of the same places on the loop: a diner, a coffee counter, a record store if one is available, a bar (rarely). He is recognized at these places and likes being recognized. He tips well — the tipping is the orphan compensating, the small dignity of being someone who can leave money on a counter. He tips in cash even when paying card.

### 7.7 Eating

Subject eats like a man who learned food from institutions and diners and never updated. Lunch is often **skipped or replaced** by coffee and a snack on the walk. Dinner is the meal he treats as social — he prefers to eat with someone present, even silently. Cooking ability is functional, not enthusiastic: he can manage a half-dozen dishes (chili, spaghetti, breakfast-for-dinner, grilled cheese, a respectable burger). He defers to Marlo on cooking when she is present.

Food preferences trend **comfort and salt**: diner food, road food, Mexican, barbecue. He does not drink soda regularly but will pour one if offered. He keeps **ice cream** in the freezer reliably across all life eras — a documented constant from the Lee era forward. Eats it from the carton, standing at the freezer door, late at night, during depressive cycles.

### 7.8 Sleep

Sleep is **irregular and shallow.** Median onset around 0030–0130; wakes once or twice per night, briefly, and listens before resettling. Sleeps on his back or his side facing the door. Will not sleep with his back to a door or window. Dreams are not in the record but the behavioral residue suggests trauma-content sleep — he wakes some mornings briefly disoriented, takes the ninety-second stillness longer.

He naps. Late-afternoon couch nap, twenty to forty minutes, common across all eras. He denies napping if asked. He naps deeper than he sleeps at night.

The hearing aids come out at night and are placed in **the same spot** every time. He cannot find them in the morning maybe one time in fifteen and becomes briefly, disproportionately frustrated — a small somatic indignity, the body's accumulating bill.

### 7.9 Grooming, Dress, and the Body

Grooming is **minimal and habitual.** Shaves every two or three days, not daily — he tolerates stubble, dislikes a beard. Hair has been worn at variable lengths across eras (long during musician phases, shorter when operational) but he cuts it himself or at the cheapest place that will do it. He has never owned an expensive haircut.

Dress: **jeans, t-shirt or button-down over t-shirt, sneakers or boots, a jacket of some kind always within reach.** A jacket on a chair-back, on a hook by the door, draped over the passenger seat. He does not own a suit he likes and owns the minimum number required to attend weddings and funerals, of which he has attended more than the average man his age. He has more **band t-shirts** than is strictly dignified for his age and wears them unironically.

He carries, every day, without exception: wallet, phone, keys, a folded multi-tool or pocket knife, a guitar pick somewhere on his person (often in a coin pocket), and — since the David era — a small notebook or its phone equivalent. He pats himself down before leaving any room to confirm these. Five-point pat: hip, hip, chest, back pocket, front pocket. Watch it once and you will see it every time after.

He moves like a man who has been hit a great deal. There is a slight favoring of one knee that comes and goes with weather. He rolls his right shoulder unconsciously every fifteen or twenty minutes — old injury, undocumented in canon but inferable from sixty years of Hulk-proximate combat. He cracks his knuckles methodically when thinking.

### 7.10 The Week as Surveillance Would Record It

A composite seven-day observation, life-phase: civilian, attached (marriage intact), no active mission.

**Monday.** Wakes 1030. Coffee, toast. Radio on. Phone check — nothing operational, two texts answered within ten minutes. Guitar, twenty minutes. Pacing the kitchen during a call with Marlo. Walk 1500–1645, standard loop, stops at the diner for coffee and pie, tips three dollars on a six-dollar tab. Home, second guitar session, this one longer, a song fragment is recorded as a voice memo and forgotten. Dinner alone, spaghetti from a jar, ice cream after. Television on for company, not watched. Bed 0115.

**Tuesday.** Wakes 0945, earlier — a call came in last night from a Tier-1 number. Behavior is now subtly different: he dresses immediately instead of moving through the kitchen in a t-shirt; the jacket is on the chair nearest the door, not the hook. He stays close to the phone all day. The walk is shorter (forty minutes) and he does not stop. Guitar is replaced by pacing. He eats whatever is fastest. The expected follow-up call does not come. He goes to bed at 0030 still half-listening for it.

**Wednesday.** Wakes 1100, later — small depressive rebound from the unconsummated mobilization of the previous day. Coffee twice. Skips breakfast. Walk is longer, two hours, varied route — he is restless. Buys nothing. Comes home, naps on the couch 1700–1740, denies it. Dinner with Marlo, eats more than usual, talks less than usual. She reads him correctly and does not press. Plays guitar in the bedroom with the door open while she reads in the next room. This is, in his life, an actual moment of rest. He does not recognize it as such.

**Thursday.** A maintenance day. Pays bills (in arrears, but pays them). Calls his bank, irritated. Replaces a guitar string. Goes to the hardware store, the grocery, the post office in a single circuit — he batches errands like a man who used to live underground. Drives himself; will not take rideshare if avoidable, an old vehicular self-reliance from the hot-rodder era. Listens to music in the car at moderate volume, sings along when alone.

**Friday.** Social. Dinner out with Marlo, possibly friends. Drinks two beers, nurses the second. Does not get drunk in public, will not. Tells one Avengers-era story with the names filed off, badly. Is recognized by someone — a fan of the memoir, a former neighbor, a barfly with a long memory — and handles it with practiced graciousness that costs him something. Comes home tired in a way that is partly performance-fatigue. Sleeps better than usual.

**Saturday.** Slow morning. Long pot of coffee. Watches a ball game with the sound low and the radio call on (a specific habit — he prefers radio play-by-play to television commentary, traceable to the orphanage and to long stretches in safehouses with only a transistor). Naps. In the evening, plays guitar for a longer session, sometimes a full hour, sometimes records something he might send to one of two old musician contacts and will not actually send. Dinner late. Stays up too late.

**Sunday.** The hardest day across all eras. Unstructured time triggers the orphan's Sunday — a low, specific dread. He compensates with **a phone call to someone in the Tier-1 circle**, almost always before noon, ostensibly casual, actually regulatory. If Banner is reachable, the call is to Banner. If not, Marlo, then in descending order down the list. The call lasts longer than necessary and ends with the three "yeahs." The afternoon is then survivable. Evening: dinner, guitar, an early bed attempt that becomes a 0200 bed.

### 7.11 The Rituals That Are Not Daily But Are Liturgical

A short inventory of observances Subject keeps without naming them as such:

- **The anniversary of the gamma detonation.** He does not mark it overtly. He is quieter that day. He calls Banner if Banner is reachable. If not, he plays guitar for longer and drinks one more beer than usual.
- **The anniversary of Mar-Vell's death.** Similar pattern, plus a specific behavior: he sits, alone, for some part of the day, and does nothing. No music, no phone, no pacing. This is the most uncharacteristic thing he does all year, which is precisely why it functions as the observance.
- **Birthdays of attachment figures.** Remembered, often the only one in the room who remembers. Calls are made. Cards, in earlier eras, were sent.
- **The reading of his own memoir.** Approximately once every two or three years, in a depressive trough, he picks it up and reads a chapter and puts it down and does not finish. He is looking for evidence that the life happened. He always finds it. It never quite suffices.
- **Visits to graves.** Variable but real. He goes alone, stays a short time, says little, leaves something small — a guitar pick has been observed.

### 7.12 What the Week Reveals

A surveillance team watching Subject for seven days without context would conclude: **a man semi-retired from an occupation he refuses to name, organized around a phone that does not ring as often as he would like, performing the daily motions of a life he is willing to inhabit but not to fully claim.** They would note the operational tells — the pat-down, the egress mapping, the door-ajar habit, the second-ring answer — and incorrectly diagnose him as a former intelligence asset gone to ground. They would not be entirely wrong; they would simply be naming the wrong agency.

The deeper finding, available only with the longitudinal record: **every routine he keeps is a placeholder for the call that reorganizes him.** The coffee, the guitar, the walk, the Sunday phone call — these are not a life in themselves, in his estimation. They are what he does between the moments that, in his private accounting, *count*. He has lived this way since he was sixteen. He will live this way until he dies, and then, given his history, possibly afterward.

*End Section 7. Section 8 (Relational Map & Attachment Inventory) follows.*


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU DOSSIER — RICHARD MILHOUSE JONES
## Section 6: Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

*The "family" file for this subject is structurally anomalous. There is no nuclear family of origin on the record — no named mother, no named father, no siblings, no extended kin, no hometown grandparents, no cousins surfacing at weddings or funerals. The biological family is a void, and the void is itself the most important fact in this section. What occupies the slots normally filled by parents and siblings are: an institution (the Scarsdale group home), a series of surrogate fathers/older brothers acquired in adulthood (handled in Relationships II), a spouse who repeatedly tries and fails to function as both peer and family (handled here as the closest analog to "family of choice"), and the dead — chiefly Mar-Vell, whose loss occupies the psychic real estate a deceased father would in any other file. This section maps the void, the institution, the spouse, and the dead, in that order.*

---

### 6.1 The Biological Parents — UNKNOWN / DECEASED / ABSENT

**Status in the record.** No names. No photographs. No grave markers cited. No recovered letters. No retconned reappearance across six publication decades — and the absence of a retcon is itself diagnostic, because the subject is exactly the kind of long-running figure to whom a returning-parent storyline would be the obvious move; every writer who has handled him has independently declined to make it. The parents are not a plot reservoir. They are a closed door, and the door is part of the architecture.

**What the subject himself has said about them.** Almost nothing, across sixty years. He does not eulogize them, does not invoke them in argument, does not name a child after them (there is no child), does not dream of them on-page, does not visit a grave, does not produce them as a wound when wounded. In the *Sidekick* memoir era under David, where he is literally being paid to excavate his life for a reading public, the parents are a single paragraph at most — orphaned young, raised in the home, end of entry. The refusal to mythologize them is total and, for a man who mythologizes every other figure he has ever attached to, conspicuous.

**Inferred dynamic.** Parents lost early enough that the subject has no operative memory of an intact dyad. The flatness of his references suggests either pre-verbal loss or a loss so early that the affective imprint never consolidated into narrative. There is no idealization (no "my mother used to sing to me"), no devaluation (no "my old man drank"), no ambivalence — there is *absence as baseline*. This is the developmental substrate on which the entire later attachment pattern is built: a person whose first model of "family" is not a relationship but a *room with other children in it.*

**Trust/safety.** N/A — never established with a primary caregiver.

**Failure mode.** The failure mode is structural rather than relational: the subject enters every subsequent attachment with no template for *being cared for as a default condition.* He has no internal working model of an adult whose job is to keep him alive without his having to earn it. Every attachment from age six onward is therefore conditional in his own mind — *I must be useful to be kept.*

**Leverage available to a hostile actor.** Minimal direct leverage (no living target, no shrine). Substantial *indirect* leverage: a sufficiently competent actor producing falsified evidence of a surviving parent, a half-sibling, a buried family secret, could destabilize the subject for as long as he believed it. He has never been tested against this vector because no one has tried it. He would be defenseless.

---

### 6.2 The Scarsdale Group Home — INSTITUTIONAL CAREGIVER

**Status.** Functioned as de facto family of origin from early childhood through mid-adolescence, terminating with the subject's transition to itinerant car-and-guitar life in the Arizona desert immediately preceding the gamma incident. No specific staff member is named in canon as a meaningful figure. No "house mother," no kindly chaplain, no foster placement that took. The institution as a whole is the parent, which is to say: nobody is the parent.

**History and dynamics.** The home appears to have provided physical safety, basic schooling, and the bare minimum of socialization. It did not provide individuated attention, attuned mirroring, or stable dyadic attachment. The subject's behavior in the immediate pre-gamma window — running with a hot-rod crowd, owning a guitar, accepting a *dare* to drive onto a live ordnance test range — is the behavior of an adolescent who has aged out of institutional containment without anyone in particular noticing or minding. He left, and the home did not chase him.

**Evolution.** The subject has never, on the published record, returned to the home. Has not visited, has not donated, has not funded a wing, has not looked up an old caretaker, has not attended a reunion, has not mentioned a single name from the place when asked to enumerate his past. For a man whose defining trait is *loyalty past reason*, the absence of any ongoing loyalty to the institution that raised him is the loudest single datum in this section. He owes a debt to a man who shoved him into a trench in 1962 that he has been paying for sixty years; he owes nothing, apparently, to the people who fed and clothed him for the first decade-plus of his life. The conclusion is that nothing there earned a debt in his accounting. The home was a holding pattern, not a home.

**Trust/safety.** Established at the floor — he was not abused enough to flinch from institutions in general (he later moves comfortably through SHIELD facilities, Avengers Mansion, military bases) but was not cared for enough to seek institutions out. Neutral imprint.

**Failure mode.** The home produced an adolescent with no model of *being chosen* — only of being *included by default and ignored individually.* This is the precondition for his later compulsion to make himself indispensable: he learned early that generic membership produces no attention, and that to be *seen* one must become *necessary*. The gamma-bomb event is, among other things, the first time in his life a single adult specifically chose to keep *him* alive at cost. The over-response to that event is intelligible only against the institutional baseline of having never been singled out for protection before.

**Leverage.** Negligible. No one from the home can be used against him because no one from the home was ever in him.

---

### 6.3 Siblings — NONE OF RECORD

No biological siblings. No fostered or institutional siblings carried forward into adult life as kin. The closest functional sibling-analogs are acquired in adulthood and belong to Relationships II: most centrally Bruce Banner (older-brother-as-burden), Bucky Barnes (the brother whose costume he wore and whose ghost he was), and Mar-Vell (twin-by-fusion). The structural absence of a sibling tier means the subject has no peer-kin template — no one with whom he learned to compete, share, ally, betray, and reconcile inside a frame of guaranteed lifelong relation. Every peer relationship he constructs in adulthood is therefore built without the sibling scaffold and reverts under stress either to *parentified caretaking* (his default) or to *fan-of* (his fallback). He cannot do *brother* in the ordinary sense because he has never practiced it.

---

### 6.4 Children — NONE

No biological children. No adopted children. No godchildren of record. The Banner-Ross union's daughter, the various young Avengers and Hulk-adjacent youth he has been around, do not occupy a parental slot in his psychology — he relates to younger heroes as a slightly-older peer or as a wry veteran, never as a father figure. This is striking. A man with his trauma load and his caretaking compulsion would, by ordinary actuarial expectation, have either had children early (to construct the family he lacked) or sought out a paternal mentee role late (to discharge the caretaking drive into a generative channel). He has done neither.

**Inference.** The childlessness is not incidental. It is the negative space left by the fact that his caretaking capacity is *fully consumed* by his attachments to giants. The Hulk is his child in everything but biology — he soothes it, he names it, he is the only person in the world who can put it to sleep, he protects it from itself and from those who would kill it, he has done this work since he was a teenager and has never been relieved of it. He has no surplus parenting to give a human infant. Marlo's reproductive history with him is not detailed on the record, but the marriage's strain around his perpetual unavailability to ordinary domestic life is consistent with a couple that has implicitly understood there will be no children because there is no room for them.

**Failure mode.** If a child of his ever surfaced — biological, retconned, falsified — the subject would attempt instant total devotion and would be psychologically unable to choose between the child and the standing obligations to Banner, the cosmic order, and the dead. He would try to do all of it and would fracture. This is a live vulnerability surface.

---

### 6.5 Marlo Chandler-Jones — SPOUSE / FAMILY-OF-CHOICE / FUNCTIONAL SISTER-WIFE-WITNESS

**Status.** Wife. Married during the Peter David *Hulk* run; the marriage has been separated, reconciled, strained, and resumed across the subsequent thirty publication years. Still legally and emotionally extant. The single longest sustained voluntary relationship of the subject's adult life with another non-powered human.

**Origin.** Marlo enters the record initially as the on-again-off-again partner of the Rhino, of all people — a woman with her own pre-existing tolerance for the orbit of the superhuman, which is the *sine qua non* of being able to remain married to this subject. She and the subject converge across a series of David-run interactions in which she is consistently characterized as taller than him, louder than him, more sexually self-possessed than him, and less impressed by his cosmic résumé than anyone else in his life. This last quality is the one that hooked him. Everyone else he has ever loved has been a god, a monster, or the keeper of a god or monster. Marlo treated him like a person.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.**

*Early phase.* The subject experiences Marlo as the first relationship in which he is the more famous, more cosmically credentialed party. He is uncertain how to occupy that position. He oscillates between performing the war stories (to justify her interest) and deflecting them (to disclaim the persona). She is amused by both. The relationship in this phase is genuinely playful — one of the few periods of recorded play in the subject's adult life.

*Marriage phase.* The wedding is itself attended by superhumans, which Marlo absorbs with composure. Post-marriage, the structural problem of the relationship emerges immediately and never resolves: the subject's primary attachments (to Banner, to the obligation-shaped hole left by Mar-Vell, to whatever cosmic event is current) are not negotiable, and Marlo is required to be the *home base* he returns to between them. She is asked, in effect, to be the only stable point in a life organized around being the stable point for others. This is a structurally exhausting role and she names it as such, repeatedly.

*Crisis phase.* Marlo is murdered and resurrected within the David run (the Trauma/Maestro material and adjacencies), an event that imprints on her a trauma history of her own and inverts the caretaking polarity of the marriage for a sustained period. The subject in this window is faced with the unprecedented experience of being the *less* damaged spouse, the one who must do the soothing. He performs creditably but visibly without the practiced ease he brings to soothing the Hulk. He has trained for monsters, not for his wife.

*Distance phase.* Across the A-Bomb transformation and the subsequent Pak/Ewing-era abductions by the Whisperer, Marlo recedes from the panel record. She is not divorced; she is *deprioritized by plot*, which mirrors the in-universe truth that the subject's life has once again been claimed by larger forces. The marriage persists as a fact, not as a daily practice.

**Trust/safety.** High, asymmetric. He trusts her completely with his interior life — she has heard the survivor-guilt monologue, the Mar-Vell grief, the Bucky-substitution shame — and she has never weaponized any of it. She trusts him with her physical safety and her loyalty, but cannot trust him with her ordinary daily presence, because his daily presence is not reliably available. The safety inside the marriage is real; the *continuity* is not.

**Evolution.** The trajectory is from playful courtship → committed partnership → trauma-saturated mutual nursing → long-haul intermittent companionship. They are now, functionally, more like long-married siblings who occasionally remember they are married. This is not a failure of love. It is the form love takes when one of the parties is the permanent on-call human conscience of the Hulk.

**Failure mode.** The marriage fails — temporarily, repeatedly, never terminally — at exactly the points where Marlo requires the subject to choose her *over* an obligation to a giant. He cannot make that choice. He can defer the obligation, he can apologize for it, he can promise reform, he cannot make the choice. Marlo knows this, has known it since before the wedding, and has chosen to remain anyway. Her own failure mode is a cyclical exhaustion that periodically forces separation and is periodically overcome by the genuine warmth that remains between them.

**Leverage.** Severe. Marlo is the single most efficient pressure point a hostile actor could apply to the subject *that does not involve Banner or Bucky*. Threats to her physical safety produce immediate, reckless, tactically degraded behavior in the subject — the one register in which his trained composure breaks. She has been used as leverage in-universe (the murder arc) and the subject's response in that period demonstrates the ceiling of his rationality under spousal threat. Any operator targeting him through her should expect maximum response and maximum unpredictability.

---

### 6.6 Mar-Vell (deceased) — FUSED OTHER / BROTHER-TWIN / SHARED BODY / WITNESSED DEATH

*Mar-Vell is biologically not family. He is included in this section because the somatic and psychic fact of years of shared embodiment via the Nega-Bands made him, in every functional sense, closer kin than any blood relation the subject ever had. He is the only person with whom the subject has literally shared a body, and his death is the closest thing on the record to the death of a parent or twin.*

**History.** The fusion arrangement: when one was corporeal in our universe, the other existed in the Negative Zone, and they swapped via the Nega-Bands. This persisted for years. The subject, then a young man, lived in alternating phases of bodily presence and bodily non-presence, with the Kree warrior occupying the other half of the wave. The experience is, in the literature of dissociation, without precedent — the subject was, for a sustained developmental window, *not continuously embodied.* The psychological consequences of this have never been clinically engaged on the record, by him or by anyone around him.

**Dynamics.** Inside the fusion, the relationship was forced-intimate to a degree no human pairing can match — each had access to the other's sensorium, memory at the edges, immediate awareness of the other's mood. The pairing produced a deep mutual fondness and, on the subject's side, a profound idealization of Mar-Vell as the noble warrior-elder-brother. Mar-Vell, for his part, treated the subject with seriousness and respect that no Earth adult had previously extended to him — he was, briefly, somebody's *partner* rather than somebody's mascot.

**Death.** Mar-Vell died of cancer — slow, civilian, undefeatable by punching. The subject was present at the deathbed in the Starlin graphic novel, in a tableau attended by most of cosmic Marvel, and watched a being he had shared a body with die of a disease that no Nega-Band could repel. This event is, in the subject's psychic economy, the death of the closest thing he has had to a father, a brother, and a twin, in one stroke. He has never produced a comparable death in narrative since because there has not been a comparable loss.

**Evolution post-death.** The subject carried the Nega-Bands and, in later David-era arcs, briefly bore the Captain Marvel mantle himself — a literal attempt to occupy the dead man's role, structurally identical to the earlier Bucky-substitution with Cap. The pattern is now unmistakable: when a powerful attachment figure dies or vacates, the subject attempts to *become* them. This is mourning conducted by impersonation. It fails each time, and each failure is itself a small re-grief.

**Trust/safety.** Was total. Cannot be re-established with anyone living, because the substrate (shared embodiment) is not reproducible.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's failure mode is that *it ended at all.* The subject's nervous system was, for a developmental window, configured to expect the periodic return of another consciousness into his body. That configuration never fully reverted. He is, at baseline, a man with a phantom limb where another mind used to be. This contributes materially to his chronic background depressive tone and to his recurrent willingness to subordinate his selfhood to a larger figure — he has lived inside one and the inside felt like home.

**Leverage.** Mar-Vell's memory is sacred ground. Invocation of him by a hostile actor — falsified messages, simulacra, Skrull impersonation (a known operational risk in this universe given Mar-Vell's history with the Skrull-Kree conflict), any whisper of *he would want you to* — produces immediate compliance the subject cannot resist. This vector has not been heavily exploited on the record and remains, in operational terms, a loaded weapon left on the table.

---

### 6.7 The Dead, Aggregate — A SHORT LITANY

Beyond Mar-Vell, the subject carries a private necrology that no formal family file would contain but that functions as kin to him. Brief entries:

- **Bucky Barnes (presumed dead for decades, later revealed alive).** Not his to mourn — he never met the original — but mourned anyway, because he wore the costume and felt the absence of the boy whose place he was filling. The later resurrection as the Winter Soldier / Whisperer converted decades of substitution-grief into present-tense obligation. Handled in Relationships II.
- **Every iteration of Banner who has been killed and replaced.** The subject has watched Banner die, return, be replaced by a Devil Hulk, an Immortal Hulk, a Maestro-tinted version, and so on. Each iteration is mourned privately and the next is loved on arrival. The flexibility required for this is, in itself, a remarkable psychological accomplishment.
- **Himself.** He has clinically died. He carries the knowledge of his own death lightly in conversation and heavily in the unconscious. It contributes to the foreshortened-future sense documented in the psychiatric section.

---

### 6.8 Summary of the Family File

The subject has no family of origin in any operative sense. The void has been filled, imperfectly and across decades, by: an institution that earned no loyalty; a wife who earns daily loyalty and is daily underserved; a dead fused-other who occupies the slot a father would; and a series of giants (catalogued in Relationships II) who occupy the slot brothers and uncles would. There are no children to carry the line and no parents to bury. The family file is, in formal terms, almost empty. In functional terms, it is the densest and most leveraged file in the dossier — precisely *because* the absences have been filled by attachments to figures whose every movement is a matter of public, planetary, and cosmic record. To map his kin is to map his vulnerabilities. To map his vulnerabilities is to map the way a decent man has survived sixty years of standing next to monsters without becoming one.

*End Section 6. Relationships II — Surrogate Kin & The Giants follows.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Dossier Section 7. Subject: Jones, Richard Milhouse. Cross-reference: Sec. 3 (Psychological — attachment), Sec. 6 (Relationships I — Marlo, Betty, family-of-origin gaps).*

What follows is the complete mapped network of Rick Jones's chosen attachments outside the marital and familial frame: the giants he has stood next to, the peers he has been allowed, the protégés he has tried to spare his own fate, and the institutional figures who tolerated, employed, or used him. Each entry is built from longitudinal observation of the behavioral record. The pattern across all of them is consistent and is the central finding of this section: **Rick does not "have friends" in the ordinary sense. He has attachment objects, each of which he tries to convert into a debt-discharge mechanism, and a small ring of true peers whose function he cannot quite metabolize because they refuse to be in his debt or to put him in theirs.**

---

## 7.1 BRUCE BANNER — Primary Attachment, Index Figure, Permanent Open Account

**History.** First contact, *Incredible Hulk* #1: Banner shoves a sixteen-year-old Rick into the instrument trench at Desert Base and absorbs the gamma blast meant for him. Every subsequent encounter between the two men is conducted on terms set in that instant and never renegotiated by Rick. He spent the immediate post-event years (1962–c.1964 publication time) as Banner's sole confidant, hiding him in caves and trailers, lying to General Ross's military intelligence apparatus on a near-weekly basis, riding shotgun in the jeep, sleeping in shifts, learning the somatic cues of incipient transformation (jaw set, breath shortening, the small flexion at the right hand). He developed, in adolescence, a tradecraft of monster-management that no other human has ever matched: lullabies; the use of his own name as a verbal anchor ("It's Rick, Hulk, it's just Rick"); the calibrated touch on the wrist; the ability to walk *toward* the Hulk at the moment everyone else runs.

Across six decades he has been Banner's roommate, conscience, biographer, betrayer (twice, both small, both regretted), and the only person in Banner's life who has known both men continuously across every iteration — Savage, Grey/Joe Fixit, Merged/Professor, Devil, Doc Green, Immortal, every fracture and re-fusion. When Banner has run, Rick has followed. When Banner has been institutionalized, Rick has visited. When Banner has died, Rick has grieved more cleanly than Betty.

**Dynamics.** Asymmetric in every visible measure of power and symmetric only in dependency. Banner needs Rick because Rick is the only human in his life whose attachment predates the Hulk and is therefore not contaminated by it; Rick needs Banner because Banner is the structural justification for Rick's continued existence. They speak in a private register — diminutives ("Doc," "kiddo," now "old man"), shared shorthand for thirty different past disasters, a willingness to sit in silence that Rick exhibits with no one else, not even Marlo. Rick is physically unguarded around Banner in a way he is around no one else: he turns his back, eats from Banner's plate, falls asleep in chairs in Banner's lab. With the Hulk specifically, he is the only person on Earth who has ever embraced the Hulk and not had it end in damage.

**Evolution.** Three phases. *Caretaker phase* (teens-twenties): Rick is the handler, Banner the burden. *Peer phase* (Peter David run, intermittent): Banner Merged/Professor, Rick married and aging; the two men sit in a kitchen and talk like equals about lives that have not gone as planned. *Inversion phase* (A-Bomb onward): Rick gamma-transformed, briefly more powerful than Banner, briefly the protector — and visibly disoriented by the role reversal, returning to sidekick patter to manage his own discomfort with the new symmetry.

**Trust/safety.** Total in both directions, with one asterisk: Rick will lie *to* Banner to protect Banner from himself (suppressing news, hiding triggers, intercepting Betty's letters during separation periods). Banner knows this and tolerates it. Rick has never lied *about* Banner to anyone who mattered.

**Failure mode.** Rick cannot say no to Banner. If Banner asks for help with something Rick believes is wrong, Rick will help and grieve afterward rather than refuse. He also cannot make Banner the bad guy in his own internal narrative — every Hulk atrocity Rick has witnessed is filed under *my fault, originally*, not *his*.

**Leverage.** Anyone who controls Banner controls Rick. Anyone who threatens Banner gets Rick's full reckless commitment. Anyone who can credibly tell Rick that Banner is "finally free of him" can induce a depressive episode lasting months. The cleanest leverage point in Rick's entire psychology runs through this man.

---

## 7.2 STEVE ROGERS — Substitute Father, Refused Substitution

**History.** *Avengers* #4: Rick is present at the Sub-Mariner-precipitated thaw of Captain America from the North Atlantic ice. Cap, disoriented and grief-saturated, sees in Rick a near-perfect physical and tonal echo of James Buchanan Barnes and offers him the Bucky identity. Rick accepts immediately — he is, by this point in his late teens, already pattern-trained on attaching to powerful grieving men by becoming their missing piece. He wears a version of the Bucky costume on early Avengers missions, fights alongside Cap, and is, briefly, the most-loved he has ever been.

Cap revokes the offer. This is the critical moment: Rogers, recognizing the unhealth of the substitution (both his own grief-projection and the moral injury of asking a teenager to step into a dead boy's shoes), retracts. He does so kindly. Rick experiences it, despite the kindness, as the second great rejection of his life after the gamma-bomb survival.

**Dynamics.** Permanently warm, permanently asymmetric, permanently haunted by what didn't happen. Cap treats Rick with a careful, almost ceremonial respect — overly formal handshakes, the use of his full first name in introductions, a habit of vouching for him in Avengers business that Rick reads correctly as compensatory. Rick performs sidekick patter at Cap more than at anyone else, because the patter is precisely the persona Cap once invited and then declined; rehearsing it is both a wound-poke and a way of saying *I'm fine, I'm fine, I'm fine.*

**Evolution.** From eager surrogate to honorary Avenger to elder statesman of the Avengers reserve list. In middle adulthood Rick and Cap stabilize into something like fond, uncomplicated comradeship — but only by mutual unspoken agreement never to discuss the Bucky offer or its retraction. When Bucky returns as the Winter Soldier (see 7.10), the unspoken thing becomes a third presence in the room whenever they meet.

**Trust/safety.** Rick trusts Cap with his life, not with his self-image. He will not be vulnerable in front of Cap; the Bucky scar is too live.

**Failure mode.** If Cap ever explicitly says *I was wrong to take it back, you would have been a fine Bucky*, Rick collapses. If Cap ever explicitly says *I was right to take it back, you were never going to be him*, Rick also collapses, differently. There is no version of that conversation that Rick survives intact, which is why neither man has ever had it.

**Leverage.** A directive from Captain America, delivered in Captain America's command voice, overrides Rick's normal threat assessment. He will charge anything Cap points at. Bad actors who have impersonated Cap have exploited this twice in the record.

---

## 7.3 MAR-VELL — Fused Symbiote, Lost Brother, the Death-Bed Vigil

**History.** Introduced as Rick's attachment object after the Cap-substitution closes. Through the Nega-Bands, Rick and the Kree warrior Mar-Vell become quantum-alternating — when one is corporeal in Earth-space the other resides in the Negative Zone, swapping by a clap of the wrists. Years of Rick's young adulthood are spent in this condition: a literal two-bodies-one-life arrangement with a man he initially barely knew, then came to know more intimately than he has ever known anyone before or since, including his wife. They share thought, dream-bleed, sensory residue. Rick learns Kree military philosophy by osmosis. Mar-Vell learns folk guitar.

The relationship ends in the most psychologically formative event of Rick's adulthood: Mar-Vell's death from cancer (*The Death of Captain Marvel*, 1982). Rick is present, conscious, no longer fused but emotionally still half-merged, watching the man he was for years half-of die slowly of a disease no cosmic power can punch. He sits the vigil. He says goodbye in a hospital room surrounded by every cosmic-tier being in the Marvel Universe, most of whom defer to him to speak first.

**Dynamics.** The closest analog in Rick's life to a sibling, or perhaps to a spouse in the older sense of *one flesh*. Unlike with Banner, the asymmetry was structural rather than personal: Mar-Vell was a powerful being but not a *monstrous* one, and the relationship was not organized around Rick managing Mar-Vell's danger. It was organized around shared embodiment. This is the only attachment in Rick's life that did not run on his caretaker reflex, which is why its loss carved a different and in some ways deeper channel.

**Evolution.** From bewildered partnership to deep fraternal intimacy to terminal grief to permanent mourning. Rick has never stopped marking the anniversary, has never spoken Mar-Vell's name lightly, and will, decades after the fact, still drift mid-sentence when the topic surfaces.

**Trust/safety.** Was total. Has not been replicated.

**Failure mode.** Rick has been manipulated more than once by figures invoking Mar-Vell's name, memory, or legacy — Genis-Vell most consequentially (see 7.4). His judgment around anything wearing the Captain Marvel mantle is permanently compromised.

**Leverage.** Posthumous. Anyone who can credibly speak as if from Mar-Vell — through legacy, through Kree authority, through claimed message — gets a hearing from Rick that no living being is granted.

---

## 7.4 GENIS-VELL — Protégé, Substitute Son, Catastrophic Failure

**History.** Mar-Vell's artificially-aged son. Inherits the Nega-Bands; the universe re-fuses him with Rick in the old configuration. Rick, now a married adult in his thirties/forties, finds himself yoked again to a young, unstable cosmic figure who is in nearly every respect a worse version of his lost partner: arrogant where Mar-Vell was disciplined, unstable where Mar-Vell was steady, eventually delusional, eventually genocidal.

**Dynamics.** Rick approaches Genis as a combination of protégé, son-substitute, and second chance at the Mar-Vell bond. He tries to mentor him — into Kree honor, into restraint, into a sense of consequence. He fails. Genis's cosmic awareness fractures his sanity; his actions become monstrous; Rick is forced into the unprecedented position of being the moral handler of a *Mar-Vell legacy* figure who has gone wrong.

**Evolution.** From hope to mentorship to alarm to horror to, eventually, complicity in Genis's containment and effective end. This is the relationship in which Rick most clearly experiences the inversion of his life-pattern: he is now the elder, the grounded one, the conscience, and his cosmic partner is the one who needs management. He hates the role and cannot refuse it.

**Trust/safety.** Eroded steadily and never rebuilt. Rick stayed past the point at which he should have left, because leaving would have been a second abandonment of the Mar-Vell line.

**Failure mode.** Rick's inability to give up on Genis cost real lives. He knows this. It is among the small set of things he cannot make a joke about.

**Leverage.** None remaining direct; but the *memory* of this failure can be wielded to induce Rick into excessive caution or into excessive intervention with any subsequent unstable powered young person who crosses his path.

---

## 7.5 ROM (THE SPACEKNIGHT) — Ally of the Long Patrol, Witness-Brother

**History.** Earth's Wraith-war years. Rick attaches to Rom during the latter's solitary, near-impossible campaign against the Dire Wraiths. He has no operational reason to be there; he simply finds Rom, recognizes another lonely figure on an unwinnable mission, and stays.

**Dynamics.** Quieter than the Banner or Mar-Vell bonds. Rom does not require management; Rom is incorruptible by construction. What Rick offers is the thing Rom most lacks — a human face, a human voice, witness. What Rick receives is the experience of being needed by a being who needs almost nothing.

**Evolution.** Fades naturally as Rom's storyline concludes and Rom departs. Rick speaks of Rom only rarely and always with a particular quiet that is unlike his register for any other ally.

**Trust/safety.** High; uncomplicated.

**Failure mode.** None observed; this is one of the few attachments in Rick's life that did not end in damage to him or to the other party.

**Leverage.** Any invocation of Wraith activity or Galadorian remnant business will pull Rick out of retirement faster than almost any other call.

---

## 7.6 THE AVENGERS (as collective) — Honorary Family, Permanent Outer Orbit

**History.** Carded as honorary/auxiliary member from *Avengers* #4 onward. Has been present at, or substantively involved in, a disproportionate number of the team's foundational crises — the Kree-Skrull War above all, where his projection of the Golden Age heroes (Destiny Force episode) was the war-deciding act. Has lived in or near Avengers Mansion intermittently across decades. Knows the cooks, the maintenance staff, the Quinjet maintenance schedule, the door codes (or used to), every iteration of Jarvis-era butler protocol.

**Dynamics.** Treated by the institutional Avengers with the specific affection accorded to mascots and family pets: real, warm, slightly diminishing. The membership has rotated entirely several times; Rick has outlasted most rosters. To newer Avengers he is a piece of furniture they have been told to respect; to founding-era survivors (Cap, Hank Pym, Wasp pre-death, Thor when he remembers) he is family.

**Evolution.** From wide-eyed teenage tagalong to seasoned hanger-on to elder-statesman emeritus. He has never been offered, and has never sought, full membership — the honorary designation suits the psychology precisely.

**Trust/safety.** Trusts the *idea* of the Avengers absolutely; trusts individual members case-by-case.

**Failure mode.** Will defer to Avengers authority even when his own judgment is better. Has been wrong about this several times.

**Leverage.** An Avengers Priority call routes around all of Rick's normal personal commitments, including to Marlo. She knows it. It is a permanent low-grade fault line in the marriage.

---

## 7.7 JANET VAN DYNE — The Sister Figure He Never Quite Names

**History.** Present at his earliest Avengers exposure; one of the few founding Avengers who never patronized him. Across decades, the warmest of his Avengers peer-bonds.

**Dynamics.** Wasp is the only powerful figure in Rick's life around whom he is reliably himself without performing the sidekick. She does not need managing, does not require him to be useful, does not project a lost brother or son onto him. She simply likes him. He does not know what to do with this and so banters less when she is in the room than when anyone else is.

**Evolution.** Stable across all eras. Survived her temporary death; the reunion was visibly significant to him.

**Trust/safety.** High; one of perhaps four people he would call before calling Marlo in a real crisis.

**Failure mode.** None observed; this is a clean relationship and the cleanness itself sometimes makes him retreat from it, because he distrusts attachments that do not cost him anything.

**Leverage.** Threats to Jan get a disproportionate response, much like threats to Marlo, but with less self-awareness.

---

## 7.8 JARVIS — Domestic Anchor, Quiet Father-Figure

**History.** Edwin Jarvis, butler of Avengers Mansion, has known Rick since Rick was a teenager eating sandwiches in the Mansion kitchen at three in the morning. Across sixty years Jarvis has fed him, lent him cufflinks, covered for him with senior Avengers, and never once condescended.

**Dynamics.** This is, on close inspection, the most fatherly relationship in Rick's life — quieter and less storied than the Banner or Cap bonds, but more consistently nurturant. Rick takes off his shoes in Jarvis's kitchen. He does not in anyone else's.

**Evolution.** Continuous. Aged together. Rick visits Jarvis when there is no operational reason to.

**Trust/safety.** Total. Jarvis is one of the very small number of people in front of whom Rick has cried.

**Failure mode.** None. Anyone who hurts Jarvis acquires a relentless and surprisingly effective enemy in Rick Jones.

**Leverage.** Threats to Jarvis collapse Rick's normal de-escalation toolkit; he becomes uncharacteristically dangerous.

---

## 7.9 HERCULES, NAMOR, THOR, AND THE OTHER OLYMPIANS — Cordial-Distant

**History.** Long acquaintance, low intimacy. Each of these figures has known Rick across his entire adult life; none has been a primary attachment.

**Dynamics.** With Hercules: drinking-companion warmth, no real intimacy, mutual liking. With Namor: wary respect on both sides; Namor is one of the few major figures who treats Rick as fully an adult, which Rick finds unnerving. With Thor: ceremonial warmth; Rick is slightly in awe and Thor reciprocates the awe in reverse, having witnessed the Destiny Force projection and never forgotten it.

**Trust/safety.** Operational, not personal.

**Failure mode.** None significant; these relationships are robust because they are shallow.

**Leverage.** Limited; these are not people whose calls override his life.

---

## 7.10 JAMES BUCHANAN "BUCKY" BARNES / THE WINTER SOLDIER / THE WHISPERER — The Returned Original, Permanent Compromise

**History.** Bucky's posthumous existence dominated Rick's adolescence-into-young-adulthood via the Cap substitution. The reveal that Bucky was alive — as the Winter Soldier, brainwashed Soviet asset — and Bucky's subsequent recovery, redemption, and turn as the off-the-books intelligence operative known as the Whisperer, reactivated every wound the original substitution had laid down. The Whisperer recruited Rick as an intelligence asset (*Captain America* / *Winter Soldier* era runs) for the precise psychological reason that Rick *cannot refuse the real Bucky anything*.

**Dynamics.** Toxic in the precise sense that the leverage is total and one-directional. Bucky knows what he is doing. Rick knows that Bucky knows. Neither says so. Bucky is, to his credit, not cruel about it — he runs Rick carefully, uses him for things Rick would approve of in any case, and protects him in the field. But the relationship is not a friendship; it is the long-deferred resolution of the Bucky-substitution wound, conducted on Bucky's terms because Bucky is the only person who could conduct it on any other terms and is choosing not to.

**Trust/safety.** Rick trusts Bucky's competence and intentions. He does not, on examination, trust his own judgment in Bucky's presence, and on his more lucid days he knows it.

**Failure mode.** Rick will run an op for Bucky that he would refuse from anyone else, including Cap. This is the cleanest single-point exploit in Rick's adult life.

**Leverage.** Catastrophic and ongoing. Any hostile actor who could turn or impersonate Bucky would have functional remote control of Rick for as long as the deception held.

---

## 7.11 BETTY ROSS (BANNER) — Sister-in-Law of the Soul

**History.** Met in the *Hulk* #1 era; co-conspirator in the long cover-up of Banner's condition; partner in grief during Banner's many disappearances, deaths, and transformations.

**Dynamics.** Bonded by shared love of the same impossible man. Rick and Betty have spent more hours sitting in waiting rooms together than most siblings spend in a lifetime. Their intimacy is real and entirely non-romantic — sexual current never developed, in part because both of them have always been organized around Banner.

**Evolution.** Stable through her marriage, her death, her Red She-Hulk period, and her returns. Through her Harpy and Red phases Rick was one of the few who continued to address her as Betty without inflection.

**Trust/safety.** High. They lie to each other only about Banner, and they both know when.

**Failure mode.** If forced to choose between Betty's wellbeing and Banner's, he chooses Banner's, and she knows that too. The marriage of the Banners has at moments required Rick to absent himself for precisely this reason.

**Leverage.** Through Banner, primarily; not independently substantial.

---

## 7.12 DOC SAMSON — Therapist, Peer, Reluctant Confidant

**History.** Leonard Samson has, across decades, served as the de facto clinician to the gamma-adjacent extended family. Rick has been in his office, formally and informally, more times than the record fully captures.

**Dynamics.** Rick deflects in session. Samson knows this. Samson lets it ride and waits for the moments Rick doesn't deflect, which come perhaps one session in five and are usually about Mar-Vell, never Banner.

**Trust/safety.** Professional trust high. Personal trust moderate — Samson is too perceptive for Rick to be fully comfortable around him socially.

**Failure mode.** Rick will lie to Samson by omission rather than commission, and Samson permits it because the alternative is Rick not coming back.

**Leverage.** Samson has not used what he knows. Anyone with access to Samson's notes (and there have been such breaches in-universe) would have a near-complete operational map of Rick's pressure points.

---

## 7.13 JEN WALTERS (SHE-HULK) — Cousin-by-Adjacency, Easy Affection

**History.** Long acquaintance via the Banner family and the Avengers; warmth without crisis.

**Dynamics.** One of the very few people in Rick's life with whom he has had genuinely uncomplicated fun. Jen does not need him, does not project on him, treats him as an ad


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*File continues from Relationships I (Banner, the Hulk, surrogate-fathers) and Relationships II (Avengers cohort, cosmic partners, found family). This section maps the antagonistic axis and the intimate-sexual axis. These categories overlap more in Rick Jones's life than in most subjects': his enemies are frequently men who tried to use the same psychological slot his protectors occupied, and his lovers have nearly all been women who refused that slot entirely.*

---

## A. RIVALS

The category "rival" sits awkwardly on Rick. He is not a competitive personality; he does not pursue zero-sum prizes. What he has instead are *position-rivals* — figures who occupied, or seemed to occupy, the relational slot he had reserved for himself beside a giant. The rivalry is almost always internal to Rick and almost never reciprocated. The rival rarely knows they are one.

### A.1. Jim Wilson

The cleanest, most painful rivalry of Rick's life — and the one he would most strenuously deny.

Jim Wilson enters the Hulk's life in the early 1970s, shortly after Rick has graduated to Captain Marvel and the Avengers orbit. He is younger, more vulnerable, a runaway, and — critically — Black, which gives Banner a relationship Rick could never have offered: the Hulk as protector of someone society had also discarded. Jim becomes the Hulk's friend in a register Rick had monopolized. Rick's behavior in the overlapping appearances is telling: he is unfailingly warm to Jim in person, and he avoids him in private. He does not visit when Jim is hospitalized with HIV (Bill Mantlo era, late '80s). He sends word. He is "busy."

*Dynamics.* On Jim's side, none — Jim regarded Rick as an elder, a Hulk-veteran, a friend. On Rick's side: displacement, mild envy, a corrosive sense that he had been *replaced as the original*, complicated by the fact that he himself had moved on first. Rick cannot allow himself to resent Jim because Jim has done nothing wrong and because Jim is dying; the affect goes underground and emerges as avoidance.

*Failure mode.* Rick was not at Jim's deathbed. He should have been. The omission is one of the few moral failures of his record and he has never spoken about it on the page. He carries it.

*Leverage.* None operational — Jim is gone. But any reminder of Jim — a photograph, an invocation by Banner — produces an immediate flattening of affect and a topic-change. The wound is live.

### A.2. Bucky Barnes

Bucky is simultaneously the most important rival in Rick's life and the man Rick most wishes to please. He occupied the slot Rick spent the entirety of *Avengers* #4–#10 trying to fill, and his return from the dead in 2005 cut the ground out from under one of Rick's three or four foundational identifications.

*History.* In 1964 Captain America, freshly thawed, casts Rick as a Bucky-substitute, dresses him in the costume, and then — when the substitution begins to feel obscene to both of them — explicitly rejects it. Rick spends years processing that rejection as a verdict on his worth; he tells himself he understood, that Cap was right, but he kept the costume in a footlocker for decades (visible in background panels in the David run). When Bucky returns as the Winter Soldier, Rick's reaction is unrecorded in any major book, which is itself the datum. He does not seek Bucky out. He does not congratulate Cap. He goes quiet for the better part of a publication year.

*Dynamics.* When they finally interact — most fully when Bucky, operating as "the Whisperer," recruits Rick as a leaker/intelligence asset — the asymmetry is total. Bucky is the original; Rick was the placeholder. Bucky knows it; Rick *especially* knows it. The Whisperer arc reads, with the psychology in front of you, less like espionage tradecraft and more like Bucky finally giving Rick the validation Cap could not: *yes, you can be useful to me, you can do what I did*. Rick agrees instantly and at enormous personal cost (abduction, exile, separation from Marlo).

*Trust/safety.* Asymmetric and unsafe. Rick will not refuse Bucky. Bucky, to his credit, appears to understand this and has used it with restraint, but the structure is exploitable and has been exploited.

*Failure mode.* Rick cannot say no to Bucky on any operationally significant request. If Bucky asks him to lie to Banner, to Marlo, or to Cap, he will lie. He has.

*Leverage.* For a hostile actor: invoke Bucky's name, fabricate Bucky's endorsement, or stage a scenario in which Bucky appears to need rescue. Rick will move before verifying.

### A.3. Doc Samson

Leonard Samson is not Rick's enemy. He is, structurally, his rival for the role of Banner's most-trusted-non-combatant. Samson has the credentials Rick lacks — PhD in psychiatry, gamma-empowered, professionally licensed to do what Rick has done amateurishly since age 17. Their interactions across the David and Pak runs are courteous, slightly cool, professionally collaborative. Rick defers to Samson's diagnoses and quietly disagrees with most of them; Samson treats Rick as a layperson whose intuitions about Banner he respects but does not credit at parity.

*Dynamics.* Samson tends to treat the Hulk as a psychological problem to be solved; Rick treats him as a person to be loved. Both are partly right. The friction is over the *frame*, never overt.

*Failure mode.* Rick under-shares with Samson information that he believes Samson will pathologize. This has, at least twice, led to Samson missing developments Rick had noticed — the Devil Hulk emergence being one — because Rick filed the observation away as "Banner stuff" rather than "clinical data."

*Leverage.* Frame Samson as having displaced Rick in Banner's confidence and Rick will produce information he would otherwise withhold, in order to re-prove his irreplaceability.

### A.4. Hercules and Namor (the alpha-Avenger axis)

A diffuse, low-intensity rivalry expressed mostly as Rick's discomfort. Both men embody an aristocratic-warrior masculinity that treats the sidekick role as inherently shameful. Hercules has been openly friendly to Rick and condescending without intending to be; Namor has been openly contemptuous. Rick reacts to both by performing more competence than he feels and by withdrawing from rooms they dominate. Neither man considers Rick a rival; Rick experiences both as judgments on his life choices.

*Leverage.* Praise from either of them lands with disproportionate weight; criticism produces an equally disproportionate spike of shame.

### A.5. Genis-Vell and Phyla-Vell

Mar-Vell's children present an unusually layered rivalry: they are the biological heirs to the man Rick was *fused with* for years, and they regard Rick — Genis especially — with a complicated mixture of curiosity, jealousy, and need. Genis treats Rick as a kind of step-uncle and tests him constantly; Phyla, more secure in her own identity, is warmer but still wary. From Rick's side: he tried to parent Genis (the Peter David *Captain Marvel* run, 1999–2004) and failed in instructive ways — he projected Mar-Vell onto the boy and was disappointed when Genis was not Mar-Vell, then guilty about the disappointment, then over-permissive in compensation. The cycle is the parentified-child structure inverted: Rick trying to be a parent, having no model for it, defaulting to *being useful* rather than being *in authority*.

*Failure mode.* Rick cannot discipline a child of Mar-Vell. He will rationalize any behavior. Genis exploited this without quite understanding he was doing so.

*Leverage.* Anything framed as honoring Mar-Vell's memory through his children bypasses Rick's judgment.

---

## B. ENEMIES

True enemies — actors with sustained hostile intent toward Rick personally — are fewer than the publication tonnage might suggest. Most of Rick's combat exposure has been collateral. The figures below are those for whom Rick is, or has been, a *named target* or a *useful instrument*.

### B.1. General Thaddeus "Thunderbolt" Ross

The original enemy, and in some ways the formative one. From 1962 forward Ross regarded Rick as the punk kid who got onto a restricted test range, caused a national-security incident, then aided and abetted a wanted fugitive across state lines for years. Ross's animosity toward Rick is the most consistent emotional through-line in Ross's published life apart from his obsession with the Hulk and his love for his daughter.

*History.* Ross attempted to arrest, draft, interrogate, detain, and at moments effectively execute Rick across the 1960s and 1970s. Rick lied to Ross's face hundreds of times. The two of them know each other's tells at a level that no other relationship in Rick's life can match — Ross can tell when Rick is hiding Banner from across a tarmac, Rick can tell when Ross is bluffing the MPs from a voice-print. It is one of the most intimate adversarial relationships in the record.

*Evolution.* Over decades the animosity decayed into a kind of forced mutual respect, and after Ross's own gamma transformation (Red Hulk) and partial rehabilitation, into something almost collegial. They will never like each other. They have stopped trying to destroy each other.

*Failure mode.* Rick reverts, in Ross's presence, to a teenager. He becomes flippant, smirking, deliberately insolent — a regression so reliable that handlers should consider it tactically.

*Leverage.* Ross knows Rick's biographical file in operational detail; in any scenario where Ross is hostile, assume Rick's location, routines, and known associates are compromised.

### B.2. The Leader (Samuel Sterns)

The Leader regards Rick the way an entomologist regards a particularly stubborn beetle — with cataloging interest and no moral consideration. Rick has been the Leader's prisoner repeatedly, his experimental subject during the *Fall of the Hulks* and *World War Hulks* arcs, and the agent of his transformation into A-Bomb. The Leader's relationship to Rick is the only one in this section in which Rick's *body* has been the contested object — Rick was kidnapped, irradiated, weaponized, and reformatted under the Leader's direction.

*Dynamics.* On the Leader's side: instrumental, intellectualized cruelty without sadistic affect. On Rick's side: the Leader is the man who took his body away from him, twice. The hatred is unusually pure for Rick, who otherwise has trouble sustaining hostility.

*Trust/safety.* Zero. Rick will not be in a room with the Leader without a contingency.

*Failure mode.* Rick underestimates the Leader's interest in him. He thinks of himself as a low-value target; the Leader has demonstrated, twice, that Rick's biography (proximate to every major gamma event for sixty years) makes him an *unusually* high-value research asset. Rick has never updated on this.

*Leverage.* The Leader has Rick's complete genetic, cellular, and neurological mapping from the A-Bomb conversion. Functionally, the Leader owns the blueprint of Rick's body.

### B.3. The Abomination (Emil Blonsky)

Blonsky's enmity toward Rick is secondary to his enmity toward Banner, but it is real, and it killed Rick once. Blonsky murdered Rick (*Fall of the Hulks* prelude, retconned) by impaling him in front of Banner specifically to wound Banner. Rick's death was instrumental — he was used as a *message* — which is its own particular humiliation.

*Dynamics.* Rick does not fear Blonsky in the way he should. He treats him as a known quantity, an old enemy from the 1960s, and consistently underweights the personal risk. The death-and-resurrection has not adjusted his behavior. This is consistent with the survivor-guilt schema: a man who believes he is living on borrowed time does not invest heavily in self-preservation.

*Leverage.* Blonsky cannot be reasoned with by Rick; any plan that requires Rick to negotiate with him fails.

### B.4. The Intelligencia (M.O.D.O.K., the Wizard, the Mad Thinker, Red Ghost)

A composite enemy, treated as one. The Intelligencia engineered Rick's abduction, conversion to A-Bomb, and death. The group regards him as a tool — a high-purity gamma substrate with a known emotional handle on Banner. The most operationally consequential observation here is that *the Intelligencia correctly identified Rick as the most efficient leverage against Banner*, and that this identification was made by a committee of supergeniuses analyzing the Hulk problem from first principles. The professional consensus of Earth's most cognitively gifted villains is that Rick is Banner's primary vulnerability. Rick himself does not accept this assessment. He should.

*Leverage.* Any of the four can be expected to attempt to acquire Rick if Banner is the target. Standing prophylaxis is warranted.

### B.5. The Whisperer apparatus (Bucky, as handler — adversarial overlay)

Bucky-as-friend belongs above. Bucky-as-handler belongs here. The Whisperer operation used Rick instrumentally — for his gamma-cleared bone marrow as an intelligence narrative, for his Avengers-historical credibility as a leaker, for his willingness — and exposed him to SHIELD's full enforcement weight. Whether Bucky regarded this as friendship or as tradecraft is unclear and probably both. The effect on Rick was: years of life burned for a cause he did not select, with a man who had once made him feel like a fraud and now made him feel, briefly, like the original.

*Failure mode.* See A.2. Rick cannot refuse this man. He must therefore be insulated from this man during any operation where Bucky's interests and Rick's well-being diverge.

### B.6. Maelstrom, Annihilus, Thanos and the cosmic-tier abstract enemies

Listed for completeness. Rick has stood adjacent to cosmic-scale threats repeatedly — Kree-Skrull War, the various Annihilation-adjacent events, the long Thanos shadow over the Mar-Vell years. None of these figures regard him as a target. Several have *noticed* him, which is its own marker — Thanos's awareness of Rick (filtered through Mar-Vell) is the single most underweighted fact in his security profile. Thanos remembers everyone who was close to Mar-Vell. Rick was closer than anyone.

*Leverage.* If Thanos ever wants something from Mar-Vell's resurrected line, Rick is on the list.

---

## C. LOVERS / INTIMATES

The intimate-sexual record is shorter than the surveillance value of it suggests. Rick is not promiscuous. He has had three serious partners and a handful of period-specific entanglements. The pattern across all of them is that *Rick attaches sexually only to women who can see through him*, and he sabotages those relationships at the exact point where the woman demands he live for himself.

### C.1. Marlo Chandler-Jones (wife)

The central relationship of his adult emotional life, and the one that taxes his organizing structure most severely.

*History.* Met during Peter David's *Hulk* run in the late 1980s — initially as Rick's rebound from Marvel-related upheaval, then escalating across years into engagement, marriage, separation, reconciliation, and a long durable partnership that has survived her death and resurrection (the Maestro/Death overlap), her possession episodes, his transformation into A-Bomb, his death, and his Whisperer-era abduction. Marlo is a former exotic dancer, taller than Rick, sharper-tongued, more emotionally honest than anyone else in his orbit. She is the only person who has consistently told him the truth about his attachments.

*Dynamics.* Marlo gives Rick the one thing his entire adaptive structure cannot metabolize: a relationship that requires him to be a *person* rather than a *function*. She does not need rescuing. She does not need to be soothed like the Hulk, grounded like Mar-Vell, or witnessed like Cap. She needs a husband. Rick has never quite learned how to be one.

*Evolution.* Phase 1 (courtship, late '80s): Marlo as escape, vivid and uncomplicated, Rick at his most playful. Phase 2 (marriage): rapid erosion as Rick's attachments to Banner and to the cosmic milieu pull focus; Marlo's tolerance high but not infinite. Phase 3 (separation and her possession/death arcs): catalytic — Rick's grief and guilt during her absence reveal that she is more central to him than he had let himself know. Phase 4 (long marriage post-reconciliation): mature, weathered, mutually accommodating, with persistent low-grade resentment on her side about being chronically third-ranked after Banner and after whatever cosmic emergency is current. Phase 5 (A-Bomb / Whisperer / death and return): she absorbs each transformation with a competence that should be impossible and is in fact slowly grinding her down.

*Trust/safety.* High on both sides regarding fidelity, candor, and basic kindness. Lower on Marlo's side regarding *priority* — she has correctly inferred that in any forced choice between her and Banner, she does not win. She has stopped testing it.

*Failure mode.* Rick disappears — physically or emotionally — when a giant calls. He does not lie about it; he simply goes. The pattern has not broken across decades. Marlo's failure mode in response is to numb rather than to leave, which is itself worrying for her long-term mental health.

*Leverage.* Threats to Marlo will override every other priority Rick has, including loyalty to Banner. This is the single most important operational fact in this entire dossier. Any actor wanting to move Rick moves Marlo first.

### C.2. Lou-Ann Savannah

The high-school sweetheart, blind, from the original 1960s Lee/Kirby run. Largely written out of the record after the early issues but never explicitly closed. The relationship is formative in a specific way: Lou-Ann is the first person Rick attempted to *protect* in the post-gamma-bomb era, and she modeled for him the way intimacy was going to work for the rest of his life — *I exist to shield this person from what I am proximate to*. She knew him before the bomb and accepted him after; that combination has not recurred.

*Failure mode.* Rick cannot return to a pre-bomb self with anyone. The relationship was outgrown by trauma, not by feeling.

*Leverage.* Sentimental only. Any unexpected contact from her (or someone presenting as her) would land hard.

### C.3. April Sommers

The older woman, his landlady during a transitional period, with whom Rick had a brief and somewhat opaque relationship in the mid-1970s. The encounter is significant chiefly because it is the first time Rick attached to a partner *not* as a caretaker — April was the older party, mildly maternal, and Rick allowed himself to be the one looked-after. The relationship did not survive his return to cosmic duty.

*Dynamics.* Brief glimpse of an alternative attachment style. Rick noticed it. He has not replicated it.

### C.4. Betty Ross (charged non-relationship)

Not a lover. Mapped here because the dynamic is intimate-adjacent and operationally consequential. Rick and Betty have been bonded for decades by their shared position as *the two non-powered humans whose lives orbit Banner*. They have wept together at his bedside, lied for him together, lost him together. The bond has never been sexualized — both would refuse it — but it is more intimate than most of Rick's actual romances. Betty is the only person on Earth who shares his particular grief.

*Failure mode.* In any scenario where Betty needs help and Banner is unavailable, Rick will respond as if she were his own family. He effectively is.

*Leverage.* Same operational handle as Banner; threats to Betty move Rick.

### C.5. The Mar-Vell fusion (intimate, non-sexual, sui generis)

Catalogued here because no other category contains it. For years Rick and Mar-Vell shared a single body across alternating realities, each conscious of the other's existence in the Negative Zone or on Earth, swapped at the clap of the Nega-Bands. This is, by any reasonable definition, the most intimate relationship in Rick's life — more intimate than marriage, because it bypassed the body entirely and made two consciousnesses tenants of one nervous system. They knew each other's thoughts, dreamed each other's dreams, and shared the somatic experience of being one person.

*Dynamics.* Quasi-fraternal on the surface, something stranger underneath. Rick's grief at Mar-Vell's cancer death (*The Death of Captain Marvel*, 1982) was not the grief of a friend; it was the grief of a *widowed limb*. He has never named it as such. He could not.

*Trust/safety.* Total, while it lasted. There has been no analog since.

*Failure mode.* No living being can occupy this slot again, which means it remains a permanent open wound and a permanent point of unguarded longing.

*Leverage.* Any apparent return of Mar-Vell — clone, simulacrum, time-displaced version, Skrull impersonator — bypasses Rick's threat assessment completely. He has been fooled by Mar-Vell-shaped figures before; he will be fooled again. This is not learnable for him.

### C.6. Pattern summary

Three observations across the intimate field:

First, Rick has *never* partnered with someone of comparable power-level or social standing within the superhuman community. His partners have been a high-school girl, a landlady, a dancer-turned-talk-show-personality, and a Kree warrior who occupied his body. The pattern is not coincidence; partnering across the power gradient would force him to confront his latent significance. He has chosen, repeatedly, partners who let him remain *small*.

Second, his most intimate bond was non-sexual, non-romantic, and somatically impossible. This is diagnostic: the central love of his life happened in a register no one has language for, and he has spent forty publication-years quietly metabolizing a grief his own culture cannot name.

Third, the marriage to Marlo is, despite everything, the closest thing to a functioning adult intimate relationship in the entire Hulk-cohort record. That it survives at all is a credit to Marlo, and to a part of Rick — the part that wants to be a husband and not a sidekick — that has never been allowed to fully come online. If anything in Rick's life will ever heal him, it will be her. He knows this. He has not, yet, acted on it.

*End Section. Continues in Relationships IV — Witnesses, Bystanders, and the Press.*


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU DOSSIER — SECTION 7
## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

*Subject: Richard Milhouse Jones. Classification: baseline-human asset, intermittently superhuman, of disproportionate operational consequence. The following inventory is organized from the most diagnostically revealing capacities outward — because in this subject the powers themselves are symptoms.*

---

### 7.1 Baseline Physiology and Conditioning

Rick Jones is, for the overwhelming majority of his sixty-year operational life, an unaugmented human male in the 5'10" / 165–175 lb range, right-handed, originally a desert-acclimated Arizonan with the lean wiry musculature of a teenage hot-rodder and the gradually thickened, slightly stooped frame of a middle-aged man who has been hit, dropped, irradiated, and concussed more times than any field record properly accounts for.

His conditioning is **incidentally elite rather than deliberately trained.** He has never run a structured combatives program. He has never held a military or law-enforcement rank. He acquired his body the way infantry acquire theirs — by being in proximity to ordnance for a long time and surviving it. The result is a man whose resting cortisol baseline is almost certainly chronically elevated, whose startle reflex to deep bass concussion (the auditory signature of a Hulk footfall) is sub-cognitive and instantaneous, and whose threat-assessment processing for *superhuman* events is faster than that of most decorated combat veterans, while his processing for ordinary civilian danger (a mugging, a traffic accident) is unremarkable. He is calibrated for the wrong world, and lives almost entirely in that world, so the calibration reads as competence.

**Cardiovascular:** repeatedly stressed past clinical thresholds, including hypoxic exposure in the Negative Zone, vacuum-adjacent cosmic events, and gamma-induced near-leukemic crisis (*Incredible Hulk* #131–135 era). Likely subclinical cardiomyopathy by middle age.

**Orthopedic:** documented fractures from blast, fall, and impact across decades; subject has been thrown by the Hulk, by Skrulls, by the Abomination, and by simple stairwells. Probable chronic lumbar and cervical pathology, with a characteristic right-shoulder favoring tell (consistent with a healed clavicle or rotator-cuff injury) visible in his guitar posture from the mid-period onward.

**Auditory:** progressive sensorineural hearing loss, bilateral, low-frequency-dominant — the signature of repeated shockwave exposure rather than industrial noise. Canonically hearing-aided in later Pak/David appearances. He has compensated since long before he was fitted, by lip-reading and by a specific forward-cant of the head when listening, which appears in artist reference as early as the Mantlo era and is now habitual.

**Neurological:** at minimum, mild traumatic brain injury accumulation. Probable mild anosmia from gamma exposure. Sleep architecture almost certainly disturbed.

He is, in plain terms, a polytrauma patient who never stopped working.

---

### 7.2 The Soothing Voice — His Single Most Consequential Skill

If one had to name the ability that has saved the most lives across the published record, it is not the Destiny Force, not the Nega-Bands, not the A-Bomb shell. It is **Rick Jones's capacity to talk the Hulk down.**

**Mechanism.** A combination of (a) prosody — low register, slow tempo, predictable cadence, never rising in pitch at moments of escalation; (b) lexical primitivism — short words, present tense, names rather than pronouns ("Hulk. It's Rick. Rick is here. Hulk is safe."); (c) physical posture — open hands, body angled obliquely rather than squared, eyes at or below the Hulk's eye line; (d) the *song* — the lullaby variant, used since the earliest Lee/Kirby issues, which functions as a conditioned auditory anchor for whichever Banner-substrate is dominant at the moment; and (e) the irreplaceable, non-reproducible variable — *Rick is the human the Hulk imprinted on first.* He is, neurologically, the Hulk's home voice.

**Range.** Effective at conversational distance; degraded but still functional by shout; fails entirely if the Hulk cannot hear him (hence Rick's catastrophic interpretation of his own hearing loss — he reads it as losing the *instrument*, not the *ear*).

**Limits.** Does not work on Hulk-forms that do not contain a Banner-substrate. Works variably on the Savage persona, well on the Joe Fixit persona (who tolerates him with affection), poorly on the Devil Hulk and the Worldbreaker. Does not work when Rick himself is dysregulated — and the Hulk reads dysregulation in him faster than any other observer.
