**Symptom-as-signature reading.** This is the parentified child's perfected technique — the boy who learned to read a volatile caregiver's weather before his own. Rick acquired this skill not from training but from necessity; he was the first person to *need* to talk the Hulk down and the only one available to try. That he is good at it is a measure of how completely he organized his nervous system around the task. The lullaby is, structurally, a self-soothing behavior he externalized onto another body. He sings to the Hulk what no one sang to him in the group home.

---

### 7.3 De-escalation, Generally

The Hulk-specific skill generalizes. Across the record Rick has talked down: Mar-Vell in psionic crisis, the Thing in a rage, a half-dozen alien warlords mid-ultimatum, the post-thaw Captain America's disorientation episodes, and at least one Skrull infiltrator who broke posture when addressed by his Skrull personal name. He has an unusual capacity for **rapid affective attunement** — sub-second pattern-matching to the emotional register of a hostile or destabilized superhuman, followed by a verbal intervention pitched precisely below the threshold of that register.

He cannot do this in writing. He cannot do this in advance. It is a real-time, in-room, body-to-body capacity. It does not transfer to politics, negotiation, or the management of his own marriage.

---

### 7.4 Tactical and Operational Skill Set

Sixty years embedded with Avengers-, Hulk-, and cosmic-tier operations have given Rick a working professional's toolkit, acquired without formal instruction:

- **Small-arms familiarity** at the level of a competent civilian shooter; he can clear and load most service pistols and several alien analogs, but is not a marksman and does not carry by preference. His distaste for firearms is consistent across the record and is, plausibly, post-traumatic — the gamma bomb was ordnance.
- **Improvised vehicle operation:** anything with a steering interface, including SHIELD craft, Skrull saucers, Kree shuttles, and at one point an Inhuman conveyance whose controls he reverse-engineered by feel. The hot-rodder origin never left him. He drives aggressively, downshifts rather than brakes, and reads engine timbre the way other operators read instruments.
- **Field medicine** at advanced-first-responder level, with specific competence in **gamma-exposure triage** that almost no other living human possesses (he has watched it happen to himself and to others enough times to know the symptom progression cold).
- **Communications and signal:** basic SHIELD-era cryptographic literacy, conversational Kree, fragments of Skrullos, fluent Hulk-speech (a genuine register, not a joke).
- **Surveillance counter-measures** at the level of a long-term intelligence asset who has been *the asset*, not the runner — he knows what handling feels like from inside it, which makes him surprisingly resistant to recruitment by anyone except the one handler he cannot refuse (see 7.10).
- **Tactical assessment:** he reads a room of superhumans for who is about to lose composure with uncanny accuracy. He reads a room of ordinary people poorly.

He is not a fighter. He has thrown punches across the record, but his hand-to-hand is brawler-grade at best and he knows it. His preferred contribution in a fight is *positioning* — getting between parties, getting civilians clear, getting the right voice to the right ear. He is, in the proper sense, a **combat medic of the affective domain.**

---

### 7.5 Music

The guitar is not a hobby; it is a regulatory organ. Rick plays at a competent club-musician level — rhythm-dominant, vocal-forward, country and folk-rock idiom, with a serviceable singing voice in the baritone range. He has gigged, recorded, and at points sustained a partial income from it. Technically he is unremarkable; **expressively he is unusually direct**, because the instrument is one of the only channels through which he addresses his own affect rather than someone else's.

The lullaby he sings to the Hulk and the songs he plays alone in a motel room are, musicologically, the same family of material. The same nervous system is doing both jobs.

He writes lyrics more readily than prose. The *Sidekick* memoir is, structurally, a long song.

---

### 7.6 The Nega-Bands and the Mar-Vell Fusion

For years — operational, embodied, continuous years — Rick Jones existed in **quantum-alternating co-embodiment** with Mar-Vell, mediated by the Kree Nega-Bands. The mechanism: clapping the bands together at the wrists initiated a swap, exchanging the corporeal occupant of normal space with the occupant of the Negative Zone. While Mar-Vell was operating, Rick existed in the Negative Zone (and vice versa), with dream-state and occasionally telepathic continuity across the swap.

**What this gave him operationally:** access, by proxy, to Mar-Vell's photonic energy projection, flight, durability, cosmic awareness, and Kree combat training — none of it under Rick's direct control. He was the *carrier*, not the wielder. The bands also gave him, when worn alone in later periods (briefly, as inheritor of the Marvel mantle after Mar-Vell's death), direct access to a subset of those powers: energy blasts, enhanced durability, flight. His personal command of these powers was always crude — he flew like a man falling sideways with intent, and his energy discharges were emotionally rather than tactically modulated.

**Limits.** The fusion was involuntary as to scheduling; Rick did not choose when to be corporeal. The bands required physical contact to swap, which created exploitable failure modes. Cosmic awareness as routed through Mar-Vell occasionally bled into Rick's dreams and produced what would clinically read as psychotic-spectrum experience without the underlying pathology — he was, briefly, hearing the universe through another man's ears.

**Symptom-as-signature reading.** Of all the things that have happened to this subject, this is the one that most precisely externalizes his internal structure. Rick spent his adolescence and young adulthood literally *not having a continuous body*, alternating existence with a more powerful man whose life he was helping to enable. The Nega-Band years are the gamma-bomb wound rendered as somatic reality. That he survived them with his personality intact is itself a measure of resilience. That he never speaks of the dissociative cost is a measure of his defenses.

---

### 7.7 The Destiny Force

The single largest power-event in the subject's record. At the climax of the Kree-Skrull War (*Avengers* #97), under extreme duress and proximity to Kree psionic technology, Rick projected the Golden Age heroes — the Patriot, the original Human Torch, the Sub-Mariner-era cohort — out of his own mind and into combat as functioning constructs that turned the engagement. The phenomenon was subsequently named the **Destiny Force** and identified as a latent psionic potential resident in humanity, of which Rick was an unusual conduit.

**Mechanism (best reconstruction).** A latent collective-unconscious tap, drawing on culturally encoded heroic imagos and instantiating them as quasi-material psychic projections, powered by the host's biological reserves and emotional fuel. Rick was not generating the heroes; he was *channeling* them. The fuel was his own.

**Range.** Demonstrated at theater-of-war scale, against Kree and Skrull capital assets. Effects persisted for the duration of the engagement and dissipated when Rick collapsed.

**Limits.** Catastrophic somatic cost: immediate unconsciousness, prolonged convalescence, near-death. **No volitional access.** Rick has never been able to reproduce the effect on demand. Subsequent flickers have appeared at moments of comparable extremity, never reliably. He cannot train into it. The Force, whatever it is, surfaces when his ordinary defenses are stripped past the floor.

**Symptom-as-signature reading.** This is the most diagnostically important power in the file. The Destiny Force is **the latent self of a man who has organized his entire conscious identity around being unimportant.** It surfaces only when that organization is annihilated — when Rick is, for once, not performing the sidekick. The unconscious knows what the conscious cannot tolerate. The reason he cannot wield it is that wielding it would require him to believe he is the special one, and his entire defensive architecture exists to prevent that belief. The power is sealed behind the wound.

---

### 7.8 The A-Bomb Form

Following gamma transformation during the Loeb/Pak era, Rick became **a blue, armored, plate-shelled Hulk-class gamma mutate**, retaining full personality and language, with strength in the lower Hulk-tier range, near-invulnerability to conventional ordnance, regenerative healing, and an exoskeletal carapace that segmented at the joints.

**Operationally:** he could trade blows with the Abomination and the Hulk himself for limited durations, survive orbital reentry, and absorb energy weapons in the low-cosmic range. He retained his voice — critically — and continued to function as the Hulk's de-escalator from inside a body that could now also stand next to him in a fight.

**Limits.** Dependent on gamma-charge maintenance; reverted under specific conditions. Carried the full neurological and metabolic risk profile of a gamma mutate. Hearing damage worsened in this period. He did not enjoy it as much as the body would suggest he should have.

**Symptom-as-signature reading.** This is the wish-fulfillment fossil. For one arc Rick got the body that could pay the debt directly — could stand where Banner had stood, take the blast he had been spared. He used it competently and surrendered it without protest when the arc closed. He did not fight to retain power. *That* is the diagnostic detail. A man whose self-concept could metabolize being a giant would have fought to keep the body. Rick let it go because it was, structurally, **the wrong kind of repayment** — it made him symmetrical with Banner rather than useful to him, and symmetry was never the point.

---

### 7.9 Captain Marvel Mantle (Brief)

Following Mar-Vell's death from cancer (*Death of Captain Marvel*, 1982), Rick inherited the Nega-Bands and operated, intermittently, as a successor Captain Marvel. Powers as in 7.6, now without the alternating fusion. He was, by his own assessment and the record's, *adequate*. He did not develop the role. He passed the mantle along without resistance when later candidates appeared.

The relevant fact is not the powers but the **gesture of abdication**. He has been handed cosmic-tier capability twice (this and the A-Bomb form) and has, twice, declined to organize a life around it. This is not lack of ambition. This is a defended structural refusal.

---

### 7.10 Intelligence Asset Capacity

In the modern period, abducted and run by the Whisperer (Bucky Barnes incarnation) as an information asset, Rick demonstrated a previously unmapped facility: **he is a natural intelligence operator.** Sixty years of being adjacent to every major super-event has given him a memorized human-network map of staggering density — names, faces, voices, tells, romantic entanglements, grudges, debts — across the superhuman population. He knows who sleeps badly, who drinks, who lies to whom about what, who would betray whom under what pressure. Almost no one else has this dataset, because almost no one has been *unimportant enough* to sit in every room and be ignored.

**Tradecraft:** acquired on the job rather than trained; competent at dead-drops, signal protocols, cover identities, and digital opsec at the level a senior handler would expect of a mid-career asset. His weakness is the one a handler would identify in five minutes: he cannot refuse the handler if the handler is *Bucky*. The compartment that contains his judgment about Bucky is the same compartment that contains the Captain America substitution wound, and it has never been closed.

---

### 7.11 What He Cannot Do

For symmetry, the negative inventory:

- He cannot **sustain a life unattached.** Empirically, across every documented period of solitude, he drifts, depresses, drinks lightly, writes, and within months reattaches.
- He cannot **wield his own significance.** The Destiny Force, the cosmic powers, the moments of being told he is the most consequential human in the room — all produce deflection, dissociation, and retreat to sidekick patter.
- He cannot **fight at superhuman tier** without external augmentation, and his augmentations have all been temporary.
- He cannot **lie convincingly to people who know him.** Marlo reads him instantly. Banner reads him instantly. The Hulk reads him faster than Banner does.
- He cannot **refuse the original debt.** Any framing that returns him to the gamma-bomb scene is operative on him at a level prior to deliberation.
- He cannot **integrate his two self-concepts** — the kid sidekick and the latent cosmic conduit — and has stopped trying.

---

### 7.12 Growth Curve

Plotted across the record, his capacity curve is not the usual upward arc of a developing operator. It is **a high baseline established almost immediately** (the de-escalation skill is functionally complete by the second year of the Hulk run), followed by **decades of broadening rather than deepening** — more languages, more vehicles, more rooms, more dead friends, more scar tissue, the same core competence. The two large discontinuities (Destiny Force, A-Bomb) are not integrated into the curve; they sit beside it like dropped tools he chose not to pick back up.

The honest assessment: he became who he was going to be at sixteen, in a trench in the Arizona desert, with another man's body between him and the light. Everything since has been the elaboration of that minute.

---

### 7.13 Summary Signature

The whole inventory, read together, describes **a man whose powers are all forms of the same act**: standing between something enormous and something fragile, and using his voice. The lullaby, the de-escalation, the Mar-Vell fusion, the Destiny Force projection, the A-Bomb shell, the intelligence-asset memory — every one of them is structurally *interpositional*. He places his body, his consciousness, or his attention between a force and a target and modulates the transmission. He is, in the technical sense, **a buffer.**

That this is also the psychological structure of his survivor guilt is not a coincidence. It is the file.

*End Section 7. Section 8 (Habits, Routines, Rituals & Tells) follows.*


## Operational Profile & Tradecraft

# OPERATIONAL PROFILE & TRADECRAFT
## Subject: JONES, Richard Milhouse

*Compiled from longitudinal behavioral observation, 1962–present. Cross-referenced against incident logs from Hulkbuster Base, Avengers Mansion sign-in records, Project: PEGASUS visitor manifests, Nega-Zone telemetry, and S.H.I.E.L.D./Whisperer asset files.*

---

### 1. DECISION ARCHITECTURE

Subject's decision-making is best described as **attachment-routed**: nearly every operational choice he makes is filtered, at speed, through the question *what does my current principal need right now?* The principal varies — Banner, Rogers, Mar-Vell, Marlo, the Whisperer, occasionally an Avengers chairperson by proxy — but the architecture does not. He is not, strictly speaking, autonomous. He is **bonded-autonomous**: capable of independent action of high quality, but only within a frame oriented around another's mission. Take the principal away and decision latency increases catastrophically; he drifts, returns to the apartment, plays guitar, doesn't shower, lets the mail stack.

Within the bonded frame his decision cycle is unusually tight. Field observation across decades shows a recurring four-beat pattern:

1. **Read the room for the giant.** Subject's first act on entering any situation is to locate the most dangerous or unstable powerful entity present and orient to it. This is not fear-driven scanning; it is target-acquisition for his **stabilizer** role. Eye-tracking, if we had it, would consistently land on the Banner/Hulk/principal within 1–2 seconds of entry regardless of who else is in the room.
2. **Assess the human cost differential.** He calculates, very quickly and very accurately, *who in this room is going to get hurt if this goes wrong and isn't enhanced enough to survive it.* He moves civilians. This is reflexive, not deliberated.
3. **Insert himself at the friction point.** Where most experienced operators would seek cover, Jones positions himself **between the principal and the threat** — frequently the threat that is the principal. He has done this with the Hulk roughly more times than anyone alive.
4. **Talk.** His final move is almost always verbal. Subject treats speech as a primary munition.

He is **risk-tolerant to the point of being risk-blind on his own behalf** and **risk-averse to the point of paralysis on others'**. The asymmetry is the tell. A normal seasoned operator weights their own survival into the calculus; Jones effectively zero-weights his. This is not bravery in the conventional sense. It is the operational expression of the survivor-guilt schema documented in §3 of the analytic basis: a body whose continued existence the subject does not believe in.

---

### 2. METHODS & TRADECRAFT

#### 2.1 De-escalation of superhuman threats — primary specialty

This is, without exaggeration, his **tradecraft**, and there is no one in the human-population register with more reps. The method, reverse-engineered from observation:

- **Voice register dropped a full octave from his speaking baseline.** When approaching a transforming or transformed Banner, subject's pitch flattens and slows; the cadence becomes near-lullaby. He does this **before** he is consciously aware he is doing it; the shift triggers at roughly the moment Banner's breathing changes.
- **Use of name, repeatedly, with no command verbs.** He says *Bruce. Bruce. Hey. Bruce.* He does not say *Bruce, calm down.* Command verbs trigger the Hulk; name-anchoring re-recruits Banner. Subject learned this empirically over thousands of trials.
- **Physical posture: hands visible, palms angled down, never up.** Palms-up reads to the Hulk as threat-assertion. Palms-down at hip level reads as containment. He worked this out by being wrong about it, more than once, in the early '60s.
- **Approach geometry: oblique, never frontal, never from behind.** He arrives at roughly a 30–45° angle off the principal's facing, slow gait, no sudden weight shifts.
- **Lullaby protocol.** Documented since *Incredible Hulk* #3-era engagements. Subject possesses a small repertoire of melodies — predominantly mid-tempo folk and early rock standards within his vocal range — that he uses as somatic anchors. The specific song matters less than the rhythm; he selects ~60–70 bpm, matching a resting heart rate he is attempting to entrain in the principal.
- **Trigger-avoidance lexicon.** He has, over time, internalized a list of words he does not say in the Hulk's presence: *Ross, soldier, monster, control, calm, sedate, capture, contain, lab.* He has on rare occasions said *Betty*, with mixed results, and only when nothing else has worked.

This skillset generalizes. Observers have noted him using structurally identical methods on Mar-Vell during cosmic-awareness overloads, on Rogers during dissociative re-entry episodes from the ice, on a destabilizing ROM during identity collapse, and on Banner's daughter when she was a juvenile. He is, functionally, a **gamma-mutate hostage negotiator with a sixty-year case file**.

#### 2.2 Witnessing

Less obvious as tradecraft, but operationally central. Subject is the **field witness** for cosmic events that no other human-tier observer survives or remembers. He has been present at: the Kree-Skrull War climax, the death of Mar-Vell (intimate proximity, hours), multiple Hulk-transformations no one else watched without intervening, several Avengers-era catastrophes. He carries the institutional memory of events that have no other living human chain of custody. He uses this. He **deploys his testimony** — calmly, factually, occasionally in print (the *Sidekick* memoir, op-ed appearances, sworn statements to the Commission on Superhuman Activities-equivalent bodies) — as an actual operational instrument when authorities are about to make a category error about a powered individual.

#### 2.3 Communications discipline

Mixed and era-dependent. In the early years, **abysmal** — he was on open radio with Banner, used real names, kept no protocol. Across the Mar-Vell years he was forced into rigorous comms by sharing a body with a Kree military officer; he absorbed Kree field discipline by osmosis. By the Avengers-auxiliary years he was competent: knew Avengers Priority codes, understood Mansion lockdown protocols, could pass himself in and out of restricted areas without incident. In the David-run era his discipline degraded again — celebrity period, talk shows, the memoir — and stabilized after his marriage to Chandler around an **everyman-grade** practice: standard phone hygiene, no encrypted devices except those issued, no operational chatter on personal lines. Under the Whisperer's handling he was upgraded to tradecraft-grade comms: drop protocols, signal/no-signal disciplines, brush-pass capability. He executed these adequately but resented them.

#### 2.4 Counter-surveillance

**Poor by training, decent by accident.** Subject does not run an SDR before a meet. He does, however, possess a developed sense for the *presence* of powered observers — he has been surveilled by Skrulls, Watchers, telepaths, and gamma-tracking sensors enough times to develop a low-grade subjective alert when scrutiny is on him. He has been wrong about this; he has also been right about it in circumstances where no instrument would have detected the watcher. We treat this as **trained intuition, not a reliable channel**.

#### 2.5 Combat

Non-powered baseline. Subject is a competent street fighter — '60s hot-rodder roots, decades of incidental brawling — with creditable improvised-weapon use, a willingness to take a punch, and a learned habit of **conceding distance to powered combatants rather than engaging them**. He does not draw firearms in principle and rarely in practice; the Nega-Bands period made him allergic to the equation of personal weaponry, and the A-Bomb period spoiled him for human-scale combat. As A-Bomb he was a Hulk-tier combatant with the moves of a hot-rodder; observers report he never quite trusted his own strength and pulled punches at the wrong moments. Reverted, he is an aging man who has been beaten by gods and survived, which leaves a particular kind of fearlessness in him that reads to opponents as either suicidal or insulting.

#### 2.6 Tradecraft tells he does not know he has

- He **touches the principal's shoulder** when leaving a successful de-escalation. Always the same shoulder (the principal's left). Diagnostic tell that he considers the engagement closed.
- He **counts exits** within four seconds of entering an enclosed space. Lips don't move; eye-pattern is consistent.
- He **arrives early to medical settings** and late to social ones. He cannot stand sitting in a hospital lobby; he will pace until called. He will keep a dinner party waiting.
- When lying — and he lies, competently, almost always to protect a principal — his hands go to his guitar callus on the left fingertips. He rubs the pad of the index finger with the thumb.
- He **closes doors behind himself with the heel of his hand on the frame**, not the knob. Habit from the group home; doors there had broken latches.

---

### 3. RESOURCES & FINANCES

Subject's resource profile is **anomalously volatile** for a man of his operational reach. He has, at various points across the file, been:

- Indigent. The Scarsdale group-home boy with a guitar case and no fixed address (early '60s).
- Quartered, fed, and effectively expensed by Banner's irregular labs, by Avengers Mansion (room and board as auxiliary), and by Kree military logistics during the Mar-Vell fusion.
- A working musician with intermittent club income (mid-'70s).
- A best-selling memoirist with significant royalties from *Sidekick* and ancillary television appearances (mid-'90s, Peter David run). This is the only period of independent wealth on record.
- Married into Chandler's household economics, which were stable but unspectacular.
- A salaried-equivalent intelligence asset under the Whisperer (off-books, cash-and-safehouse model).

He is, throughout, **bad with money in a specific way**: not extravagant, not addicted, but **structurally unable to plan a financial life he doesn't believe he'll live to need**. The survivor-guilt schema expresses itself fiscally as a refusal to accumulate. He gives money away. He underbills. He has, on multiple documented occasions, signed over royalties or honoraria to causes connected to Banner, to Mar-Vell's memory, or to families of bystanders harmed in incidents he considers his fault. The *Sidekick* royalties were, per his own statements, substantially redirected.

**Infrastructure he reliably has access to**, formally or informally:

- **Avengers Mansion / Tower** — auxiliary-tier access, lapses and restores by era and chairperson; he has clearance for non-classified levels and is generally tolerated above his nominal grade.
- **Banner's safehouses** — he knows most of them. He is one of perhaps three people alive who could find Banner in under 48 hours if Banner did not want to be found.
- **Project: PEGASUS / Gamma-research community** — known and admitted; treated by gamma scientists as a useful informal liaison.
- **The Kree diplomatic-residual network** — legacy from Mar-Vell; thin, but he has names.
- **Whisperer / Barnes network** — operationally active in the current era; he has dead-drops, a handler, and a phone he does not own.
- **A guitar.** Treat this as infrastructure. It is the longest continuously-held object in his possession and he uses it instrumentally — as identity anchor, as social lubricant, as a literal de-escalation tool with both the Hulk and a juvenile She-Hulk on separate occasions.

**Documents and identity.** Subject has held legitimate ID for most of his life; has on occasion been issued Avengers ID, Kree military credentials (during fusion), and at least one set of clean papers from the Whisperer network. He has been declared dead and resurrected on paper, which has produced **chronic, low-grade administrative chaos** in his financial and legal life that he manages by ignoring it.

---

### 4. PATTERNS OF ACTION

#### 4.1 Daily and weekly rhythm (between principals, stable period)

- Wakes early — 0530–0630 — regardless of bedtime. Group-home conditioning.
- First action: phone check for messages from current principal. If none, second check at the half-hour. He will check three times before accepting the principal is dark.
- Coffee. Black, strong, percolated rather than brewed; older equipment by preference.
- Local foot circulation for one to two hours: same diner or two diners, same newsstand, same record store or its successor format. **Route-redundant** but not consciously varied. A surveillance team would have him in a week.
- Afternoons: guitar, correspondence, light reading (popular nonfiction, biographies, road literature; **not** comics, **not** physics).
- Evenings: with Marlo when together, alone when not. Television. He watches sports without strong allegiances.
- Sleep onset is **late and difficult**; he reads in bed. Has been observed sleeping with the light on more often than off.

#### 4.2 Operational tempo (engaged period)

When attached and active, the schedule collapses. He sleeps when the principal sleeps, eats when he can, and demonstrates **prolonged operational endurance well above his physiological baseline** — fueled by the bond, depleted at the bond's release. The post-operation collapse is reliable: 12–36 hours of near-unresponsive sleep, followed by 3–10 days of low-grade depression he does not name as such.

#### 4.3 Travel

Subject travels **light, fast, and without paperwork when he can avoid it**. Preferred mode in the modern era is rail; he distrusts air travel since one particular Avengers-quinjet incident and prefers ground. Hotels chosen by feel — mid-tier chains in walking distance of a diner. He pays in cash when he has it. He registers under his real name; he has never been good at aliases and does not pretend to be.

#### 4.4 Information-gathering

He runs an informal HUMINT network of **bartenders, diner staff, mechanics, low-grade radio operators, retired SHIELD personnel, and ex-Avengers auxiliary staff** that is geographically distributed, durable across decades, and almost entirely off-paper. He maintains it through small kindnesses, not money: he remembers names, asks after children, sends postcards. This is **the single most underrated capability in the file.** A subject who has been kind, consistently, to the same people for forty years has built an intelligence net of remarkable density without ever calling it that.

---

### 5. SIGNATURE OPERATIONAL TELLS

These are the patterns by which a trained observer would identify subject's hand in an event without needing to see him:

- **A Hulk-incident with anomalously low civilian casualties and no kills.** When Banner is in the field with Jones nearby, the casualty curve flattens. Statistically demonstrable across the incident log.
- **A powered fugitive turning themselves in voluntarily, calm, and with a coherent statement.** Subject's signature; he has talked at least eleven powered individuals into surrender across the file.
- **Lullaby, mid-incident, on a comms channel that wasn't supposed to be open.** Diagnostic.
- **A bystander being moved out of a blast radius by a non-powered man in jeans and a flannel or denim jacket who then runs *toward* the event.** Recurring witness-statement pattern.
- **A handwritten condolence note to a family of an incident victim, arriving within a week, no return address, signed only "R.J."** Documented in at least nineteen cases.
- **A guitar case in an inappropriate location.** Avengers debrief rooms. PEGASUS clean rooms. Once, a Kree warship. He brings it. He does not always play it. He brings it.
- **A man who calls the principal by their **non-codename, first-name diminutive** — Bruce, Steve, Mar — in front of subordinates who would not dare. Subject is one of the few humans alive licensed by long use to address Avengers-tier figures this way, and he does it casually enough that the licensing is invisible until you notice no one corrects him.**
- **At Mar-Vell's grave on the anniversary, every year, without exception.** He has missed birthdays, anniversaries, and his own book launch. He has not missed this. If an adversary wanted him at a known location on a known date, this is the date.
- **The phrase "I'm just—"** as a sentence-opener. He uses it before *the kid, the sidekick, a friend, here to help, passing through*. It is his verbal signature and his deflection in one. Anyone wishing to manipulate him should listen for it and then refuse the diminishment; refusing it disorients him and opens him.

---

### 6. OPERATIONAL VULNERABILITIES (for the file)

Catalogued because the dossier requires it, and because anyone with this file in their hands should understand the asymmetry between his usefulness and his exposure.

- **He will go anywhere a recognized principal asks him to go.** This is the single largest exploit surface in the file. Impersonation of Banner, Rogers, Barnes, or the residual Mar-Vell signature would route him into a trap with high reliability.
- **He cannot be paid to betray a principal.** He can, however, be **persuaded that the betrayal serves the principal**. Frame matters. Anyone trying coercion-by-payment will fail; anyone running a long social-engineering play that re-routes his protective instinct will succeed.
- **Marlo Chandler is the leverage point of last resort and the one he is least prepared to defend against.** He has built no operational discipline around her because doing so would require admitting she is in the line of fire, which would require admitting he is.
- **The anniversary at Mar-Vell's grave.** Re-stated for emphasis. Predictable. Annual. Unmissed.
- **The word *Bucky*, spoken in a voice he believes belongs to Barnes.** Documented compliance vector.

---

### 7. SUMMARY CHARACTERIZATION

Subject operates as a **bonded human stabilizer for super-human systems**, with a tradecraft developed empirically over six decades of unprecedented field exposure and never formally trained. His methods are voice-first, geometry-second, violence-last. His resources are thin and his network is wide. His financial life is a refusal disguised as carelessness. His tells are warm, repetitive, and human in a way that would be sentimental in any other file but in this one constitute the actual operational instrument: he is effective **because** he is the same man, in the same flannel, with the same guitar, calling the same giants by their first names, year after year, until they remember who they are.

He is not a hard target. He is not, in the conventional sense, a target at all. He is a **fixed point**, and the operational question the file should be asking is not how to acquire him but how to ensure that the fixed point is not removed — because the incident log on the other side of his removal is the dossier no one wants to compile.

*End Section. Cross-reference: §3 (Psychological), §4 (Psychiatric); see also forthcoming Section on Relational Mapping for principal-by-principal exposure analysis.*


## Worldview & Operational Code

# WORLDVIEW & OPERATIONAL CODE

*Section 7 of the longitudinal file. Belief structure reconstructed from sixty publication-years of observed behavior, recorded speech, internal monologue (where canonically rendered), and the consistency of his choices across regimes, writers, and crises. What follows is the architecture of how Richard Milhouse Jones reads the world and acts within it - the inherited axioms, the earned theorems, the working rules he runs on without naming.*

---

## 1. THE METAPHYSICS — How He Believes The World Is Built

### 1.1 The Two-Tier Cosmos

Rick's universe is, in his bones, a two-tier place. There are **giants** - the beings around whom physics, history, and consequence cluster - and there are **people**, who live in the weather the giants make. He does not consider this an injustice; he considers it a fact, the same way a man on a coast considers tides a fact. His entire adult life is organized around the seam between the two tiers, and he has elected himself a kind of customs officer of that seam.

This is not the worldview of a hero-worshipper. A hero-worshipper believes giants are *better*. Rick believes giants are *larger* - which is a different and more useful conviction. Larger means more consequential, more dangerous, lonelier, and more in need of a witness. It does not mean morally superior. He has watched gods behave like children and monsters behave like saints often enough to keep the two axes - scale and virtue - rigidly uncrossed in his mind. This is, quietly, one of the most sophisticated things about him.

### 1.2 Consequence Is Conserved

He believes, without ever having articulated it, that consequence is a conserved quantity. The blast that should have killed him went into Banner instead; it did not disappear. A debt was not erased, only transferred. The Nega-Band years taught him the same lesson in a different dialect: when he was corporeal, Mar-Vell was in the Negative Zone, and vice versa - presence is zero-sum, and somebody is always paying. This is a metaphysics with no free lunches and no clean exits, and it is the deep grammar beneath his survivor guilt. He would call it common sense. It is, in fact, a coherent and unusual cosmology.

### 1.3 Decency Scales — Sometimes

He believes, against considerable evidence, that the right word delivered to the right monster at the right moment can change an outcome. He has the data to support a more cynical position - he has been beaten, betrayed, used as an asset, abducted, irradiated, killed - and yet he has retained the conviction that *some* fraction of catastrophes are interruptible by a human voice. He believes this because he has interrupted enough of them to keep the hypothesis alive: the lullaby that brought the Hulk down, the words that grounded Mar-Vell, the speeches that have steadied Cap. The conviction is empirical, not religious. He would discard it if it stopped working. It has not stopped working often enough to discard.

### 1.4 Power Without Conscience Is The Only Real Evil

His moral cosmos is unusually simple at the top level and unusually fine-grained beneath it. At the top: power without a conscience attached to it is the supreme danger of existence. Beneath that single axiom he is highly granular - he distinguishes between malice, negligence, indifference, narcissism, despair, and possession, and adjusts his approach to each. But the master rule is the axiom: every concentration of power requires a conscience riding shotgun, and where one is missing, one must be supplied. He has, by long default, appointed himself that conscience whenever a slot opens.

### 1.5 The Universe Is Not Hostile, But It Is Indifferent And Crowded

He does not believe the cosmos is out to get him. He believes it is **populated** - busy, layered, occupied by intentions far larger than his own - and that staying alive in it requires not paranoia but *attention*. This distinguishes him from the truly traumatized hypervigilants, who experience the world as actively predatory. Rick experiences it as a place where, if you do not pay attention, something enormous will step on you without noticing. He pays attention. He has been stepped on anyway. He keeps paying attention.

---

## 2. THE SELF-LOCATION — Where He Believes He Stands

### 2.1 The Bridge Theory Of His Own Existence

If asked to state his function in plain language - and he has been, in interviews within the fiction, in his memoir, in private conversation with Marlo - he would say something like *I am the human in the room*. He believes his job, the one that justifies the fact of his continued breathing, is to translate between the giants and the people: to humanize the cosmic to itself, and to bear witness for the ordinary in rooms where the ordinary do not get a vote. He understands this is not a heroic function in the conventional sense. He believes it is necessary anyway.

### 2.2 The Debt As Permanent Address

He does not believe the debt incurred in the gamma trench can ever be paid in full. He has tested this belief against decades of service and found it intact. He believes, instead, that the debt is something more like a **permanent address** - a location at which he can be reached, the moral coordinates from which his life is conducted. This is not despairing. He has made a kind of peace with it; he no longer expects to be free of the obligation, and has stopped trying. The peace is incomplete - the debt still aches in him during depressive periods - but it is durable enough to function as a foundation.

### 2.3 Replaceability As The Existential Threat

He believes the worst thing that could happen to him is not death (he has died) and not pain (he has lived in pain) but being told by a giant he serves that he is *not needed*. This is the load-bearing terror. He organizes his life to prevent it, and he is uniquely vulnerable to anyone who can deliver or threaten it. He knows this about himself, in a half-acknowledged way - he has seen the pattern in himself often enough to name it during sober conversations - but knowing it has not loosened its grip.

### 2.4 The Refusal Of His Own Significance

He genuinely does not believe he is a consequential figure in his own right. This belief is held against substantial evidence - the Destiny Force episode, the Captain Marvel mantle, the A-Bomb period, the memoir, the sheer number of cosmic-tier events at which he was decisive - and he refuses the evidence with a consistency that is itself a kind of work. The refusal is not modesty. It is structural: if he is significant on his own, then the debt-architecture of his life collapses, and he cannot tolerate that collapse. So he keeps the architecture standing by refusing to let the evidence in. This is one of the few places in his belief system that operates against the data, and he knows it operates against the data, and he keeps doing it anyway.

### 2.5 The Ordinary As Vocation

He believes, with an unfashionable seriousness, that ordinariness is a vocation - that being the un-powered human in a room of demigods is itself a *role* with duties and dignities attached. He does not romanticize it (he is too tired for that) and he does not perform it (he is too genuine for that). He simply believes the function exists and that he occupies it. This is the closest thing in him to a religious vocation; he would never use that language about it.

---

## 3. CONFLICT — How He Reads And Engages It

### 3.1 The De-escalation Bias

His first move in any conflict is de-escalation, and this is not cowardice but doctrine. He believes most superhuman conflicts are misunderstandings amplified by force projection, and that the cheapest intervention is almost always verbal and almost always early. He intervenes early when he can. He has learned, expensively, that late de-escalation requires more body than he has, and so he reads situations forward in time aggressively - what will this look like in four minutes, in fifteen, in an hour - and tries to inject the word that prevents the punch.

### 3.2 The Lullaby Doctrine

The Hulk-soothing technique is the operational kernel of his entire conflict philosophy, and he has generalized it. The doctrine: **find the frightened thing inside the dangerous thing, address the frightened thing in a voice it remembers from before it became dangerous, and the dangerous thing will, briefly, hold still.** He believes this works on the Hulk, on Mar-Vell during the cancer terror, on Cap during the post-thaw disorientation, on Marlo during her dissociative episodes, on himself during his own bad nights. He has tested it on cosmic beings with mixed results. He keeps testing.

### 3.3 The Tiered Combat Calculus

He believes there are three tiers of conflict and three corresponding modes of engagement:

- **Human-tier** (a bar fight, a domestic, a mugger): handle directly, with the body, without escalation, and with the working assumption that the other person is probably reachable.
- **Enhanced-tier** (powered combatants, trained operators, super-criminals): do not engage with the body unless cornered; engage with information, position, or by routing the conflict to a more capable ally. The body is for delay, not decision.
- **Cosmic-tier** (gods, abstracts, civilization-level threats): the body is irrelevant; the only contribution is presence, witness, and the occasional word that lands. Survive long enough to be in the room when the word is needed.

He moves between these registers smoothly and without ego. He does not try to fight cosmic threats with his fists, and he does not call in Avengers for a mugging. This calibration is sixty years deep and almost entirely unconscious.

### 3.4 Violence As Last And Loud

He is not violence-averse in the squeamish sense - he has been around violence his entire adult life, has absorbed it on his own body, has watched it administered to people he loved. He is violence-*reluctant* in the strategic sense: he believes violence is the loudest and least precise instrument available, and that using it preempts every quieter instrument you might have used instead. When he chooses violence he chooses it without hesitation, and he chooses it knowing he has run out of better options. This is a mature posture, not a moralistic one.

### 3.5 The Asymmetry He Lives In

He understands, in granular detail, the asymmetry of his own combat position: he is a non-powered human standing next to people who can punch through buildings. He has constructed an operational identity that does not pretend otherwise. His contributions are positional (be in the right place), informational (notice what the giants miss), tonal (set the emotional weather of a room), and witness-based (be the human who saw it, so the event has a human observer on record). He does not believe he needs to land the punch. He believes he needs to be there when the punch lands, and to make sure the right people understand why.

---

## 4. CONTROL — His Beliefs About Power, Agency, And Constraint

### 4.1 Control Is Largely An Illusion, And This Is Good News

He does not believe he controls much. His index trauma taught him at fifteen that consequence outruns intention - that you drive onto a test range as a joke and a man's life ends - and he has never recovered the illusion of mastery that most people maintain into middle age. He considers this clarity, not damage. He believes people who think they are in control of events are usually about to be embarrassed.

### 4.2 What He Believes He Can Control

He believes he can control: his **proximity** (where he stands), his **tone** (how he speaks), his **attention** (what he notices), his **loyalty** (whom he stays with), and his **word** (what he commits to). That is the entire list, and he holds it with rigor. Everything else he considers contested, contingent, or simply not his to administer. This list is the working surface of his agency, and within it he is precise.

### 4.3 The Refusal Of Power As A Theological Position

When power has come to him - the Nega-Bands, the Captain Marvel mantle, the Destiny Force, the A-Bomb form - he has not enjoyed it. This is not affectation. He genuinely experiences the holding of large power as a violation of his proper position, and he returns it to its rightful holder or sheds it as soon as the situation permits. He believes power belongs to the people built for it and that he is not one of those people. Whether this belief is accurate is debatable; the firmness with which he holds it is not.

There is a secondary, less acknowledged component: holding power dissolves the debt-architecture. If he becomes one of the giants, he can no longer pay the giant. He cannot tolerate that dissolution. So he sheds the power and returns to his post.

### 4.4 Authority Versus Power

He distinguishes sharply, and instinctively, between authority and power. Cap has authority and proportionate power; he respects both. Ross has authority without proportionate moral weight; he respects neither. Banner has enormous power and intermittent authority over it; Rick treats Banner's authority as the relevant variable and helps to bolster it. Mar-Vell had authority that exceeded his power; Rick gave him the supplementary body. This taxonomy runs in the background of every interaction he has with consequential people, and he calibrates his deference accordingly.

### 4.5 Institutional Skepticism, Not Anti-Institutionalism

He does not trust institutions, but he does not hate them. He has watched the U.S. military hunt his best friend across continents for decades on the basis of a fear he considers legitimate and a tactic he considers wrong. He has watched SHIELD, the Avengers as a corporate body, the Kree, the Skrulls, and assorted intelligence apparatuses behave with varying mixtures of competence and self-serving cruelty. He believes institutions are necessary, that they tend toward corruption at a predictable rate, and that the only useful posture toward them is **cooperative skepticism** - work with them where they are doing the right thing, walk away when they aren't, and never confuse their interest with one's own. He is not an ideologue about it. It is just what he has seen.

---

## 5. TRUST — His Operational Theory Of Other People

### 5.1 The Two-Stage Trust Model

He extends a kind of *operational* trust quickly and a *deep* trust slowly. The operational trust is what allows him to function in the field with strangers; it costs him little and is easily withdrawn. The deep trust - the kind he extended to Banner inside the first hour of knowing him - he gives rarely and irrevocably. Once a person is inside his deep-trust circle, he does not revoke the trust even when they have given him cause. He absorbs the cause and keeps the trust. This is sometimes mistaken for naivety. It is in fact a deliberate position - he has decided that the cost of being occasionally betrayed by people he loves is lower than the cost of becoming a person who revokes love under pressure.

### 5.2 The Read

He reads people quickly and well, especially under pressure. His pattern recognition for human and quasi-human psychology is the result of sixty years of being the un-powered observer in rooms full of powerful, unstable people; he has had to develop it to stay alive. He can tell within minutes whether a stranger is dangerous, whether a friend is lying to spare him, whether a giant is about to break. He under-rates this skill - he experiences it as obvious - and consequently does not always articulate to others what he has noticed.

### 5.3 Loyalty As Supreme Virtue, And Its Costs

Loyalty is, for him, the cardinal virtue, and he believes most other virtues are second-order. He has the courage to admit this is partly a self-justifying position - loyalty is what he has to offer, so of course he ranks it first. But he believes it anyway, on its merits: in a universe of giants and storms, the only stable point of reference is who showed up and stayed. He believes this with a Malcolm-X-grade clarity about the costs of disloyalty in a hostile world, though without any of the political ideology - his is a private ethics, not a public one.

The cost: he has stayed with people past the point of his own interest, repeatedly, and he knows it. Marlo has, more than once, asked him to revise the ranking and put their marriage at the top. He has tried. He has not entirely succeeded. The ranking is older than the marriage and it will probably outlast it.

### 5.4 The Betrayal Tolerance

He has an unusually high tolerance for betrayal, and a low tolerance for *contempt*. Betrayal he understands - people get scared, get bought, get broken - and he can almost always find a frame in which it is forgivable. Contempt is different. He cannot work with someone who looks down on him, and he can spot the look from across a room. This is not pride; it is the calibration of a man who has spent his life being condescended to by his betters and has decided which condescension he will and will not absorb. From Banner, Cap, Mar-Vell, he absorbs almost anything. From a stranger who thinks he is *just the kid*, he absorbs nothing.

### 5.5 The Whisperer Vulnerability

He believes - and this is one of the few beliefs he holds against his own better judgment - that he cannot refuse the original Bucky anything. This is not loyalty in the standard sense; it is a more specific obligation, rooted in the years he spent occupying Bucky's costume and identity. He believes he owes the man whose ghost he wore. He knows this is exploitable. He has been exploited on it. He has not revised the belief.

---

## 6. RISK — How He Models And Tolerates It

### 6.1 The Asymmetric Wager

His risk model is asymmetric in a way that distinguishes him from most operators. He treats risk to himself as cheap and risk to others as expensive, and he prices them in different currencies. His own life he treats as a borrowed resource (it was, technically) that should be spent on outcomes that matter. Other people's lives he treats as ends in themselves. This produces behavior that looks reckless from the outside and is in fact ethically consistent from the inside.

### 6.2 Recklessness Has A Ceiling

He is reckless about *his body* and conservative about *the mission*. He will charge a Hulk-tier combatant if it buys time; he will not allow a poorly-planned operation to proceed because someone's ego is invested. The distinction is sharp and visible in his decisions across decades. He has talked Cap and Banner both out of bad calls; he has thrown himself into worse ones without comment.

### 6.3 The Acceptance Of Polytrauma

He has accepted, at a deep operational level, that his body will continue to be damaged. He does not flinch from this. He believes the body is the instrument with which the work is done and the instrument will wear out; the question is whether the work was worth the wear. He has not concluded the work was unworthy. He has, on bad nights, wondered if he chose the work because the wear was the point.

### 6.4 The Time Horizon

His planning horizon is short to medium - hours to weeks, occasionally months. He does not plan in years. He believes long-range planning is for people whose lives are their own, and his is not. This is not nihilism; it is allocation. He will commit a decade to a marriage or a friendship; he will not commit a decade to a personal project. The memoir was an exception, and it nearly broke him to finish.

### 6.5 Catastrophic Risk Versus Chronic Risk

He is well-calibrated for catastrophic risk (he has lived inside it) and poorly calibrated for chronic risk (he discounts it). The cumulative gamma exposure, the cardiovascular cost of decades of Hulk-adjacent combat, the slow erosion of the marriage - these are the risks he under-prices, because they do not look like the risks he was trained, by his life, to recognize. He responds to a charging Wendigo more competently than to a slow Wednesday.

---

## 7. KNOWLEDGE, TRUTH, AND HOW HE THINKS

### 7.1 Empiricism Of A Practical Kind

He does not theorize. He observes, tests, retains what works, and discards what does not. His beliefs are almost all back-derived from experiences, and he is quick to update them when experience contradicts them - with the conspicuous exceptions noted above (his own significance, the Bucky obligation, the debt). Within his areas of competence he is one of the better naturalists of human and superhuman behavior in his world.

### 7.2 Anti-Intellectual Self-Presentation As A Defense

He performs less intelligence than he has. This is a defense developed over decades of standing next to Banner and Mar-Vell, men whose intellects make his own irrelevant to the conversation. Rather than try to compete on terrain where he must lose, he established an alternate registry - common sense, street smarts, the wisecrack - that he can win on. The performance has become partly real: he genuinely thinks of himself as the dumber person in most rooms he occupies, when in fact his pattern recognition and tactical sense often outrun the geniuses around him. He would not believe this if told.

### 7.3 Truth As A Loyalty Object

He believes in telling the truth to the people he loves, even when it costs - and especially when it costs him. He will lie to outsiders, institutions, and adversaries without compunction (the lies to Ross are decades long and elaborate), but he holds an almost religious line on truth-telling inside his trust circle. The *Sidekick* memoir was an extension of this: a public truth-telling that he believed he owed, even though the writing of it was a depressive exercise that did him visible harm.

### 7.4 Memory As Moral Practice

He remembers, in fine detail, what happened. He believes forgetting is a form of disrespect to the dead and to the survivors. He carries the gamma trench, the Nega-Band years, Mar-Vell's death, every Avengers-tier event he witnessed, with a clarity that is partly traumatic and partly chosen. He believes the witness function he has appointed himself requires the memory function, and he maintains the memory deliberately. This is costly. He has decided the cost is part of the job.

---

## 8. HIS PLACE — The Operational Code In One Page

If the dossier had to reduce his working code to the lines he runs on, they would read something like:

- **Stay near the giant.** Proximity is the first contribution. Be in the room.
- **Be useful before you are asked.** The bond is maintained by need, and need is maintained by usefulness.
- **Find the frightened thing inside the dangerous thing.** Address it in the voice it remembers.
- **Take the body damage. Do not take the mission damage.** Your body is borrowed; the mission is real.
- **Tell the truth to your people. Lie to everyone else as needed.**
- **Loyalty is the first virtue. Everything else is downstream.**
- **Do not accept power. It dissolves the debt and the debt is your address.**
- **Notice what the giants miss.** They have scale. You have attention. Trade in the asymmetry.
- **Remember everything.** Forgetting is disrespect.
- **The right word at the right moment changes outcomes. Sometimes. Keep speaking anyway.**
- **You are not the special one. (Even when you are.)**
- **When in doubt: be the human in the room.**

This is the code. He has not written it down. He runs on it anyway, and has run on it consistently across six decades, multiple deaths, several bodies not his own, and every regime change in his world. The consistency is the diagnostic finding. The man has a philosophy. He just would never use that word for it.

*End Section 7. Section 8 (Daily Rituals, Routes, And Somatic Tells) follows.*


## Moral & Ethical Framework

# UATU DOSSIER — RICHARD MILHOUSE JONES
## Section 7: Moral & Ethical Framework

*The enacted code, derived from sixty years of behavior under observation. What he professes is largely irrelevant; what he does under pressure is the file.*

---

### 7.1 The Operative Code in One Sentence

Stripped of self-deprecation and the wisecracking register, Rick Jones operates from a single principle: **"If I am alive, someone paid for it; my job is to make sure the payment was not wasted, and to stand between the people who pay and the powers that take."** Every ethical decision he makes can be traced back to this proposition. It is not a philosophy he has articulated — it is a metabolism.

---

### 7.2 The Foundational Axioms (As Enacted)

Across the entire record, his behavior under pressure consistently observes the following rules, in roughly this hierarchy of override:

1. **Witness the death.** When someone he is attached to dies, he is *present*. He does not flinch, leave the room, or look away. Mar-Vell in 1982 is the cleanest case — he sat at the bedside through a cancer death that the cosmos itself attended — but the pattern recurs across the record. He believes, without ever saying so, that the dying are owed a witness, and that the witness must be the person who loved them. He will defy security cordons, military quarantine, and cosmic protocol to enforce this.

2. **No civilian gets eaten by the monster.** When the Hulk is in motion and bystanders are in the field, Rick will physically place himself between them and the creature. He has done this hundreds of times. It is not bravery in the heroic register; it is bookkeeping. The original civilian on the test range was *him*; everyone else's life is now on the same ledger.

3. **The conscience must be present in the room.** Where great power is being deployed — by Banner, by Mar-Vell, by Cap, by the Avengers, by Rom, by the Whisperer — Rick believes someone must be there whose only job is to say *no* or *stop* or *that's enough*. He has appointed himself this person and has never resigned the post. He will say it knowing he will not be obeyed; he believes the saying matters anyway.

4. **Loyalty is non-negotiable and non-transferable.** Once attached, he does not detach. The Banner attachment has survived betrayals, rampages, the Maestro timeline, the various splits and merges of Hulk personalities, and Banner's own multiple attempts to push him away. The Cap attachment survives Cap's rejection of the Bucky-substitution. The Mar-Vell attachment survives death itself and continues to organize behavior decades later. He treats loyalty as a covenant, not a feeling.

5. **The truth, eventually.** He lies tactically — to Ross, to authorities, to journalists — to protect the people in his orbit, but he does not lie *about* them to himself, and he does not lie permanently. The *Sidekick* memoir is the clearest expression: at a certain point the record must be set straight, even at the cost of friendships, even at the cost of his own dignity. He believes the public is owed an accurate version of the giants, eventually.

6. **Decency scales.** This is the strangest axiom and the one that most distinguishes him. He genuinely believes that a kind word to a rampaging god-tier monster can change an outcome. The lullaby to the Hulk is the prototype. He has tried this approach with Galactus-adjacent entities, with the Abomination, with Thanos-adjacent threats, with various cosmic intelligences. He is right often enough that the practice has not been beaten out of him.

---

### 7.3 What He Will Do

**He will lie to the State.** From the earliest years, Rick has lied to General Ross, to military intelligence, to S.H.I.E.L.D., to congressional inquiries about the Hulk, and to a long sequence of subsequent security organizations. The lying is consistent in shape: it protects the giant, it protects the civilians the giant would otherwise threaten by being hunted, and it accepts personal legal jeopardy as the cost of doing business. He has been detained, interrogated, threatened with treason charges, and has never given up Banner.

**He will physically interpose.** He puts his unaugmented body between superhuman combatants and the people they would hit. The cumulative orthopedic record (see §2) is the evidence; he has done this so often it is a reflex rather than a decision.

**He will surrender his own body.** The Nega-Band fusion was voluntarily renewed across years. The various gamma exposures included at least one he chose. The A-Bomb transformation he initially resisted but came to accept as a way to be useful at the new scale his friends operated at. He treats his own corporeal continuity as expendable in a way that should be alarming and is.

**He will speak truth to power inside the room.** He has told Cap he was wrong, told Banner he was being a coward, told Mar-Vell he was being arrogant, told the Avengers collectively that they were being stupid. He does this softly, usually with a joke as the carrier wave, but he does it. The wisecrack is the delivery mechanism for criticism he could not otherwise muster the social standing to deliver.

**He will accept being used.** When the Whisperer recruited him as an intelligence asset, Rick understood he was being used and went anyway, because the user was Bucky and because the work was the work. This is a moral choice he has made repeatedly: he will allow himself to be instrumentalized by people he believes are net-good, and will absorb the personal cost of the instrumentalization without complaint.

**He will tell the story.** The memoir, the interviews, the willingness to be on the record about the cosmic events most witnesses suppress or are silenced about — Rick believes the historical record matters and has acted as an unauthorized historian of the Marvel-cosmic strata for most of his life. This is an ethical commitment, not a vanity project; the *Sidekick* book damaged several of his most important relationships and he wrote it anyway.

---

### 7.4 What He Won't Do

**He won't kill.** Across sixty years of proximity to lethal violence, the kill count is essentially zero in the deliberate column. He has been in positions where killing would have been tactically rational and has declined. This is not squeamishness; he has watched and participated in horrors. It is a held line. He believes killing is the giants' work and refusing it is part of being the human in the room.

**He won't abandon the attachment object, even when he should.** The inverse of his loyalty axiom. He has stayed in proximity to Banner through events that would have driven any sane handler away; he has stayed available to Cap, to Mar-Vell's memory, to the Avengers, past the point of being asked. Marlo has named this as the problem in the marriage and she is correct. He will not detach, and he knows this is sometimes a moral failure rather than a virtue.

**He won't keep a secret that protects a giant at a civilian's expense.** The asymmetry is precise: he lies to power *for* the powerful when the lie protects the public from collateral damage. He will not lie *for* a giant when the lie sacrifices a specific bystander. This line has held across multiple Hulk-related coverups; when a civilian death was caused, Rick has gone on the record.

**He won't take credit.** Offered formal Avenger status, offered the Captain Marvel mantle as a permanent identity, offered acknowledgment for the Destiny Force summoning, he has consistently deflected, minimized, or returned the recognition. This is partly the survivor-guilt schema (§3) and partly a held principle: credit is for the giants, witness is for him.

**He won't weaponize the lullaby.** The trust the Hulk places in him is, in a strict tactical sense, the single most valuable lever in the Marvel-cosmic security architecture. Multiple parties across the decades have tried to acquire it. Rick has never sold it, traded it, or used it against Banner's interest. Not once. This is the firmest line in the entire record.

**He won't claim the cosmic.** When told he is special, latent, the Destiny Force, the secret protagonist, he refuses the identification. This is not always a virtue — sometimes it is a defense — but it functions as an ethical commitment to staying human-scale, refusing apotheosis on principle.

---

### 7.5 The Justifications (As He Tells Them To Himself)

He almost never articulates his reasoning. When pressed, the justifications collapse into a handful of stock phrases delivered in the wisecracking register: *"Somebody's gotta," "He's my friend," "It's the right thing, you know how it is," "Look, I was there."* These are not arguments; they are placeholders for an internal logic he has never had to formalize because he has never been asked to defend it by anyone whose opinion he respected.

When he does articulate — in the *Sidekick* memoir, in the long-form interviews of the David era, in private with Marlo — the justifications cluster around three themes:

- **The debt.** "Banner saved my life. I owe him." Delivered flat, unembellished, as if it were a fact about gravity.
- **The witness function.** "Somebody has to remember what happened. Somebody has to be the one who was there." This is the rationale for the memoir, for the historian role, for the refusal to look away from deaths.
- **The conscience function.** "These guys, when they're in it, they need somebody to remind them who they are." This is the rationale for the lullaby, for the interpositions, for the willingness to be in rooms where he has no business being.

What he does *not* say, ever, is the deeper truth the behavior reveals: *I do this because if I do not, the fact that I am alive becomes intolerable.* He has never spoken this aloud in the published record. It is the engine.

---

### 7.6 Where The Code Is Consistent

The astonishing thing about the longitudinal record is how *stable* the code is across writers and decades. Lee, Thomas, Englehart, Starlin, Mantlo, David, Pak, Ewing — none of them coordinated, and all of them independently produced a Rick Jones who:

- will lie to authority to protect the monster
- will not lie when the lie costs a bystander
- will not kill
- will not abandon
- will not betray the lullaby trust
- will refuse credit
- will sit beside a dying friend

This convergence across uncoordinated observers is the strongest possible evidence that the code is *real* — not an artifact of any one author's reading but a structural property of the subject's psychology that any honest observer recovers.

The code is also consistent across scale. Rick treats the obligation to a civilian on the street and the obligation to a cosmic entity with the same moral weight. He does not scale his ethics to the importance of the parties. The lullaby for the Hulk and a quiet word for a frightened bystander are the same act in his internal accounting.

---

### 7.7 Where The Code Fractures

The fractures are precise and worth cataloguing, because they are where leverage exists.

**Fracture 1: Marlo.** The code organizes around obligations to the giants and to the public. It has no clean rule for the obligation to *the equal partner who asks him to exist for his own sake*. Marlo is the structural anomaly in the system. He fails her not because he loves her less but because his ethical operating system has no instruction set for the relationship she actually offers. He has tried, repeatedly, to invent one, and has repeatedly defaulted back to the giant-and-witness architecture as soon as a giant calls.

**Fracture 2: Bucky.** The "won't be weaponized" line holds against everyone except the actual Bucky Barnes. When the Whisperer activated him, Rick complied past the point where his own code would normally have refused. The substitution wound (§3) is the override — Rick is uniquely incapable of refusing the original to whom he was the replacement. This is the single most exploitable fracture in the entire framework, and any sophisticated adversary who recovered the Bucky vector would own him.

**Fracture 3: The A-Bomb period.** When he became powerful in his own body, the code briefly destabilized. The witness-and-conscience role does not transfer cleanly to a being who can punch at the relevant scale. He drifted, made decisions he would not have made as the human in the room, and the moral clarity that characterizes the rest of the record dimmed. He was happier and worse. The arc resolved when he returned to roughly human scale; the lesson he took, and has not articulated, is that *power corrupts him specifically because it dissolves the role his ethics depend on*.

**Fracture 4: The Destiny Force.** When he is forced to confront his latent cosmic significance, the witness ethic conflicts with the no-credit ethic, and both conflict with the conscience-must-be-present ethic (which would, if applied to himself, require him to *use* the power). The conflict resolves into dissociation and deflection. This is the cleanest case in the record of his ethical system being unable to compute its own inputs. He goes silent and changes the subject.

**Fracture 5: The lying.** The rule is "lie to authority, not about specific civilian harm." In practice, the line between *protecting Banner from the State* and *covering up specific harms Banner caused* has been hard to hold, and the record contains several cases where Rick stretched the rule past where it should have gone. He has gone back, years later, and corrected the record in most of these cases — the *Sidekick* memoir performs much of this correction — but the original lies were ethical failures by his own standard, and he knows it.

**Fracture 6: The reckless interposition.** "No civilian gets eaten by the monster" is held with such ferocity that it has occasionally cost him strategic positioning that would have protected *more* civilians downstream. The local interposition is satisfying to the conscience and not always optimal for outcomes. He has not corrected this in sixty years; it is a held value, not a failure, but it is a place where the ethics he enacts diverge from the ethics that would maximize the good.

---

### 7.8 Shifts Over Time

**Phase 1 (Adolescence, ~1962–1968): Pure reparation.** The code is entirely organized around Banner. Lying, interposing, devotion are reflexive and unexamined. There is no developed ethical theory; there is a debt and an attempt to pay it. Decisions are made within minutes of the trigger and almost never revisited.

**Phase 2 (Substitution and fusion, ~1964–1982): Generalization to other giants.** The Cap-Bucky episode generalizes the code from a Banner-specific obligation to a *role* obligation — Rick discovers he can be the human in the room for *any* giant, and the code expands to cover the new attachments. The Mar-Vell fusion makes the code somatically intimate; ethical and corporeal life merge. Witness function emerges as a distinct value during this period, crystallizing in the deathbed at the end.

**Phase 3 (Post-Mar-Vell collapse, ~1982–1987): Code in stasis without an object.** Without a primary attachment, the code has nothing to operate on. He drifts, half-attaches to several figures, and the ethical system is in a kind of dormant state. The behavior in this period is more morally ambiguous than at any other time in the record — not because he became worse, but because the system requires a giant to organize around.

**Phase 4 (The David maturation, ~1987–1998): Code articulated.** The marriage to Marlo, the Captain Marvel mantle, the *Sidekick* memoir — Rick begins, for the first time, to *think about* his ethics rather than just enact them. The code is largely the same but is now self-aware. The truth-eventually axiom is operationalized as the memoir. The Marlo fracture is consciously identified. He develops, late, the capacity to be ashamed of specific past decisions, which is an upgrade in moral functioning even when it is painful.

**Phase 5 (A-Bomb and after, ~2008–present): Code stressed by power.** Becoming a gamma-mutate stresses the code because the role it depends on is no longer available. The drift in this period is the diagnostic data. The eventual reversion and re-stabilization show that his ethics are *role-specific*, not abstract — he is a deontologist of position rather than principle.

**Phase 6 (Whisperer era and current): Code captured by the Bucky vector.** The Fracture 2 problem becomes the dominant dynamic. He is doing largely ethical work, but the consent architecture around it is compromised. He knows this and has not yet found his way out.

---

### 7.9 Synthesis

The honest summary: Rick Jones operates one of the more coherent and durable ethical systems in his world, derived not from study or doctrine but from the metabolizing of a single early trauma into a lifelong vocation. The code is consistent enough that uncoordinated observers across six decades reproduce it identically. It has known fractures — Marlo, Bucky, power, his own significance — and an unspeakable engine. It has never required him to be cruel and has rarely required him to be brave in the way the giants are brave; it has only required him, daily, for sixty years, to remain in rooms he could leave and to stand between people who do not know he is standing there.

He would not describe any of this as ethics. He would call it showing up. The file notes that this is, in its way, an ethical position.

*End Section 7.*


## Communications & Linguistic Profile

# COMMUNICATIONS & LINGUISTIC PROFILE: RICHARD M. JONES

*Surveillance-grade linguistic mapping. Compiled from longitudinal audio-textual record (1962–present), cross-referenced against handler-account, journal, memoir (*Sidekick*, ghost-edited), broadcast appearances, intercepted personal correspondence, and SHIELD/Avengers debrief transcripts where available.*

---

## 1. BASELINE REGISTER

Subject's default speech is **mid-Atlantic American casual**, with a vestigial Southwestern flatness on the back vowels (the Arizona group-home origin survives in his /æ/ and his unmarked rhotic R) that surfaces under fatigue or emotion. He has lived in New York, Los Angeles, and aboard a Kree warship; the accent has been sanded by exposure but not replaced. Cadence is **quick, percussive, mildly anapestic** - he tends to pile two unstressed syllables before each stress, which produces the characteristic "rattling" delivery that interlocutors across decades have described as "kid talk" even when he is in his fifties.

Pitch sits in the mid-tenor range, with a habitual upward inflection at the end of declarative sentences that reads as questioning even when he is stating fact. This **uptalk is a deferential marker, not a generational one** - it predates the cultural pattern and persists in him long after his peers have abandoned it. Functionally it asks permission to have spoken. He does it less around Marlo, more around Banner, most around Captain America. Around Mar-Vell, during the Nega-Band years, it disappeared almost entirely - one of the few periods in which his speech sat flat and confident, attributable to the somatic certainty of the bond.

Speech rate accelerates under stress in a way that is **diagnostically reliable**: at rest, approximately 140-150 wpm; in combat or under interrogation, climbs above 200 wpm with concurrent reduction in pause length and a marked rise in filler density. He becomes more articulate, not less, under acute physical danger - a learned register from decades of needing to talk down Hulk-level threats in seconds. The voice that emerges in those moments is older, lower, and **noticeably free of uptalk**. This is the operational Rick, and he disappears the instant the crisis ends.

---

## 2. LEXICAL FINGERPRINT

### Anachronistic slang as identity-armor

The single most consistent feature across sixty years of recorded speech is his **deliberate retention of dated American slang**. He continued saying *cool it*, *daddy-o*, *the most*, and *gone* well past their natural lifespan; in the 1970s he switched into *bread*, *heavy*, *trip*; in the 1980s/90s adopted some surfer-Cali residue (*bogus*, *no way*) from a Los Angeles period; in the present era he speaks in a strange palimpsest of all of these, sprinkled with current usage he picks up unreliably and slightly off-target. He says *based* wrong. He uses *literally* in the older sense.

This is not arrested development. It is **identity preservation through diction**. The slang of his teens is the verbal architecture of the person he was the day before the bomb - the hot-rodder with the guitar and no debt - and he conserves it the way a refugee conserves a dialect. Strip it out and there is no continuous self linking 1962-Rick to present-Rick except the trauma. The slang is the bridge.

### Diminutives, possessives, and the naming pattern

He **nicknames compulsively** and asymmetrically. Banner is *Doc* (originally and durably; only switches to *Bruce* in moments of intimate concern or when scolding). The Hulk, in direct address, gets soft second-person constructions and his actual name: *Big guy. Hey, big guy. It's me.* Captain America is *Cap*, never *Steve* in public, sometimes *Steve* alone. Mar-Vell was *Marv* in private, *Captain* in mixed company - the only attachment-figure he addressed by rank as a sign of respect rather than distance. Marlo is *Marl* in tender moments, *Marlo* when sober and frightened, *babe* in the David-era marital arguments, never anything cutesy.

Note the directionality: he **gives nicknames downward in the power gradient never, upward always**. He has no nicknames for civilians. He has nicknames for everyone who could kill him. This is a propitiation marker - the soft name is the linguistic equivalent of an outstretched palm.

### Self-reference

He refers to himself in third person more than is healthy: *That's Rick Jones for you. Rick Jones, sidekick to the stars. Story of Rick Jones's life.* The construction is **always self-deprecating, never self-aggrandizing**, and almost always inserted at the end of a turn as a tag. It is the verbal equivalent of stepping backward out of the frame after speaking.
